The Problem of Composite Subjects in Quantified Propositions
Author addresses the problem of composite subjects in quantified propositions and offers solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a composite subject like 'every rational animal' cannot be the subject of a quantified proposition and evaluate the proposed solutions.

Core Idea
A quantified proposition's subject must be a simple universal, but composite subjects break the classification.

In this passage, the author tackles a logical puzzle: if the subject of a quantified proposition must be a simple universal, then propositions like 'Every rational animal is human' seem to fall outside the classification. He explores possible solutions, including reinterpreting 'universal' or focusing on the beneficial part of the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement
The author states that the subject in a quantified proposition must be universal (kullī). This means it must be a simple universal, not composite.
Universal here means a concept applicable to many individuals.
2
Spot the problem
Consider the proposition 'Every rational animal is human'. Its subject 'rational animal' is composite (hayawān nāṭiq). The author asks whether it qualifies as quantified.
It is not singular, natural, or unquantified either.
3
Check the alternatives
The author notes that this proposition is not singular (shakhṣiyya) because the subject is not a specific person. It is not natural (ṭabīʿiyya) because the judgment is on individuals, not the nature. It is not unquantified (muhmala) because 'every' quantifies it. So a missing division occurs.
4
Examine the solutions
Two solutions are offered: either 'universal' here means something different (including composite), or the real subject is the core meaning (al-mufīd) without the restricting part.
The phrase 'it is said' indicates these are possible resolutions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهره كغيره ان الموضوع في المحصورة يجب ان يكون كلياً فعليه يلزم ان لا يكون نحو قولنا كل حیوان ناطق انسان مما الموضوع فيه مركب غير جزئى من المحصورات و لاشك انه ليس بشخصية ولا طبيعية ولا مهملة ايضاً فيلزم الواسطة وعبارة المصنف سالمة من ذلك كما لا يحق فان ما لم يكن مشخصاً ولانفس الحقيقة يشمل المفرد والمركب. اللهم الا ان يقال: ان مرادهم بالكلى هينا حيث جعلوه مقسماً للمحصورة والطبيعية و المهملة غير ما هو المصطلح فيما بين القوم ولا يخفى بعده، أو يقال: ان الموضوع فى المثال المذكور و نحوه هو المفيد دون المجموع المركب منه ومن القيد فتأمل. ثم اعلم انه جعل الطبيعية قسماً و مقابلاً لما حكم فيه على الافراد لا لما بين فيه كمية الافراد كما فعله الكاتبي حيث قال موضوع الحملية ان كان شخصاً معيناً سميت مخصوصة و شخصية و ان كان كلياً فان بين فيها كمية افرادها عليه الحكم كلا أو بعضاً ايجاباً أو سلباً سميت محصورة و مسورة و ان لم يبين فيها كمية الافراد فان لم يصلح لان يصدق كلية وجزئية سميت طبيعية و ان صلحت لذلك سميت مهملة لما فيه من شايبة توهم ان الحكم فى الطبيعية ايضاً على الافراد لكنها لم يبين كميتها بناء على ما هو المحقق عندار باب المعانى من ان النفى اذا دخل على جملة توجه النفى على قيد زايد و بقى اصل المعنى بحاله فقولنا : «ما جائنى زيد قائماً مثلاً ينفي قيامه لا مجيئه ايضاً. :لا يقال يحتمل ان يكون قوله : و ان لم يبين فيه كمية الافراد سالبة منتفية الموضوع، فانها كما تصدق بانتفاء المحمول تصدق بانتفاء الموضوع ايضاً وسيجيء انشاء الله تعالى. لانا نقول: هذا لا يجوز بالنسبة الى المهملة فانها قد يحكم فيها على الافراد قطعاً، بل الجواب ان يقال: ان المراد من قوله: «و ان لم يبين فيها كمية الافراد مفهومه الأعم الشامل لوجود الموضوع وانتفائه فيصح بالنسبة الى الطبيعية و المهملة ولا يخفى ان ذلك ليس استعمالاً للملفظ في المعنيين بل في المعنى الاعم الشامل لهما فتامل. (میرزا محمد علی)
Its apparent meaning, like others, is that the subject in a quantified [proposition] must be universal. Accordingly, it follows that something like our statement 'Every rational animal is human' would not be among the quantified [propositions], since its subject is composite and not particular. And there is no doubt that it is neither singular nor natural nor unquantified either, so a middle term is necessary. Yet the author's expression is free from that, as is not the case, for what is not specified and not the essence of the reality includes the simple and the composite. Unless it is said: What they mean by 'universal' here, where they make it a divisor of the quantified, natural, and unquantified, is different from the terminology among the people, and its remoteness is not hidden. Or it is said: The subject in the mentioned example and the like is the beneficial part, not the composite whole from it and the restriction. So reflect. Then know that he made the natural a division and opposite of that which is judged about individuals, not of that in which the quantity of individuals is stated, as al-Kātibī did when he said: The subject of a categorical proposition, if it is a specific person, is called 'particular' (makhṣūṣa) and 'singular' (shakhṣiyya); if it is universal, then if its quantity of individuals is stated in relation to the judgment, all or some, affirmative or negative, it is called 'quantified' (maḥṣūra) and 'quantified by quantifiers' (musawwara); and if the quantity of individuals is not stated, then if it is not apt to be true as universal and particular, it is called 'natural' (ṭabīʿiyya); and if it is apt for that, it is called 'unquantified' (muhmala), because of the suspicion of imagining that the judgment in the natural is also about individuals, but its quantity is not stated, based on what is established in the science of meanings: that when negation enters a sentence, the negation is directed to an additional restriction and the original meaning remains. So our saying 'Zayd did not come standing' negates his standing, not his coming. It cannot be said: It is possible that his statement 'and if the quantity of individuals is not stated' includes a negative with a negated subject, for it is true by the negation of the predicate as it is true by the negation of the subject as well, and it will come soon, God willing. For we say: This is not permissible regarding the unquantified, for it may be judged about individuals absolutely. Rather, the answer is to say: What is meant by his statement 'and if the quantity of individuals is not stated' is its more general meaning that includes the existence and non-existence of the subject, so it is valid for the natural and the unquantified. And it is not hidden that this is not using the word in two meanings but in the more general meaning that encompasses them. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage tackles a difficulty: if the subject of a quantified proposition must be a simple universal, then a proposition like 'Every rational animal is human' has a composite subject, so it would not be quantified, but it is not singular, natural, or unquantified either, leading to a missing division. The author offers possible solutions: either 'universal' here is used in a sense different from the standard, or the subject in such examples is the core meaning (al-mufīd) without the restriction. The author then reviews al-Kātibī's classification, noting that the natural proposition is defined by judgment on the nature, not by quantity, and addresses an objection about negative propositions with non-existent subjects. The final answer is that the phrase 'quantity is not stated' has a general meaning including both existence and non-existence, covering both natural and unquantified propositions without implying multiple meanings.

A) The predicate is not universal.
B) The subject is composite and not a simple universal.
C) It is a natural proposition.
D) The quantity is not stated.

Key Takeaway
For a quantified proposition to be valid, its subject must be a simple universal; composite subjects create a classification problem that requires careful resolution.

Al-Kātibī's Classification of Propositions and the Natural vs Unquantified Distinction
Author explains al-Kātibī's classification and resolves a potential ambiguity regarding negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a proposition as singular, quantified, natural, or unquantified according to al-Kātibī, and explain the resolution for negative propositions with non-existent subjects.

Core Idea
Al-Kātibī classifies propositions by whether quantity is stated and whether the nature can be universally or particularly true.

The author reviews al-Kātibī's classification, which divides categorical propositions based on the subject. Natural propositions are those where judgment is on the nature itself, not on individuals. An objection arises regarding propositions with non-existent subjects, and the author resolves it by noting that 'quantity not stated' has a broader meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Kātibī's schema
Al-Kātibī says: if the subject is a specific person, it's singular (shakhṣiyya). If universal, then if quantity stated (all/some), it's quantified (maḥṣūra). If not stated, then see if it can be true as universal and particular: if not, it's natural (ṭabīʿiyya); if yes, it's unquantified (muhmala).
Natural is judgment on the nature, not on individuals.
2
Distinguish natural and unquantified
Natural proposition: the judgment is on the nature itself, not on individuals. It is not apt to be true as universal or particular. Unquantified: apt to be true both ways.
Example: 'Man is rational' is natural? Actually 'Man is an animal' could be universal. The key is whether the nature can be predicated of individuals.
3
Address the objection about negative subjects
An objection: what if the proposition is negative and the subject is non-existent? For example, 'No man is standing' could be true even if no men exist. The phrase 'quantity not stated' might be interpreted as including such cases. But the author argues that for unquantified, it is about individuals, so non-existent subjects are not covered. The answer: 'quantity not stated' has a general meaning that includes existence and non-existence of the subject, so it covers both natural and unquantified without ambiguity.
The author uses the example of negation entering a sentence to show that negation affects a part, not the whole.

Source Text

(میرزا محمد علی)

The Four Types of Propositions and the Defense of Ibn Sina's Threefold Division
Explains the four proposition types and defends Ibn Sina's threefold division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of propositions and explain why Ibn Sina's threefold division is still exhaustive.

Core Idea
Four proposition types exist, but Ibn Sina's three are sufficient for scientific discourse.

In classical Islamic logic, propositions are classified based on their subject. This lesson introduces the fourfold division—singular, natural, quantified, and unquantified—and addresses the criticism that Ibn Sina's threefold division (dropping the natural proposition) is incomplete. You will learn why later logicians expanded the division and how Ibn Sina's view is defended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Look at the four terms: singular (شخصية), natural (طبيعية), quantified (محصورة), unquantified (مهملة). What is the basis for each?
The basis is the nature of the subject.
2
Understand the definition of each
For singular, subject is a real particular. Natural: judgment on the nature itself. Quantified: quantity specified (universal/particular). Unquantified: quantity omitted but still considered.
The unquantified is considered a proposition despite missing quantity.
3
Recognize the historical context
Ibn Sina in al-Shifā' divided propositions into three, excluding the natural. Later logicians criticized him for lack of exhaustiveness.
This passage is part of a commentary defending Ibn Sina.
4
Analyze the defense
The defense argues that natural propositions are not considered in the sciences, so their exclusion does not break exhaustiveness. The divisor (proposition) does not include natural ones, so the division covers all that it should.
Examine the logic: exhaustiveness requires covering all members of the divisor.
5
Apply the classification
Given a proposition from an Islamic science (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'), determine its type and explain why.
This step prepares you for real usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انما سمى الاولى شخصية، لان الموضوع فيها امر مشخص :ای جزئى حقيقى، والثانية طبيعية لان الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة لاعلى افرادها، والثالثة محصورة، لان افراد موضوعها محصورة على سبيل الكلية والجزئية، والرابعة مهملة، لان فيها كمية افراد موضوعها. ثم اعلم ان تقسيم القضية الى الاربعة من محدثات المتأخرين وقسمها الشيخ في الشفاء الى اقسام ثلاث فاسقط الطبيعية عن الاعتبار و شنع عليه المتأخرون بعدم الانحصار. والجواب ان الكلام فى القضية المعتبرة والطبيعيات لا اعتبار لها في العلوم كما سيجيء بيانه فخروجها عن التقسيم لا يخل الانحصار لان عدم الانحصار بان يتناول المقسم شيئاً لا يتناوله الاقسام و المقسم لا يتناوله الطبيعيات فلا يختل الانحصار بخروجها . (عبد الرحيم)
The first is called singular because its subject is a specific thing, i.e., a real particular; the second is called natural because the judgment is upon the nature itself, not upon its individuals; the third is called quantified because the individuals of its subject are limited in terms of universality and particularity; the fourth is called unquantified because the quantity of the individuals of its subject is [stated] in it. Then know that the division of the proposition into four is from the innovations of the later scholars. The Shaykh [Ibn Sina] in al-Shifā' divided it into three divisions and dropped the natural from consideration, and the later scholars censured him for lack of exhaustiveness. The answer is that the discourse is about the considered proposition, and natural propositions have no consideration in the sciences, as will be explained. So their exclusion from the division does not impair exhaustiveness, because lack of exhaustiveness is when the divisor includes something that the divisions do not include, and the divisor does not include the natural [propositions]. So exhaustiveness is not impaired by their exclusion. (Abd al-Rahīm)
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage explains the names of the four proposition types: singular (real particular subject), natural (judgment on the nature), quantified (quantity specified), and unquantified (quantity not specified but still considered). It then notes that this fourfold division is a later innovation; Ibn Sina in al-Shifā' divided propositions into only three, omitting the natural proposition. Later logicians criticized him for this, arguing the division is incomplete. The author defends Ibn Sina by arguing that the discussion concerns propositions that are considered in the sciences, and natural propositions are not considered in the sciences because they treat the nature without regard to individuals, so their exclusion does not break the exhaustiveness of the division.

Key Terms
شخصية (shakhṣiyya): Singular proposition with a real particular subject.
طبيعية (ṭabīʿiyya): Natural proposition where the judgment is on the nature itself. [Note: Ibn Sina excluded it; later logicians included it.]
محصورة (maḥṣūra): Quantified proposition with specified quantity.
مهملة (muhmala): Unquantified proposition where quantity is omitted but the proposition is considered.
الشيخ في الشفاء (al-Shaykh fī al-Shifāʾ): Ibn Sina's work al-Shifā', where he divided propositions into three, excluding natural. [Note: Historical reference.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of proposition did Ibn Sina recognize in al-Shifā'?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The fourfold division of propositions is an innovation, but Ibn Sina's threefold division remains logically exhaustive when natural propositions are excluded from scientific consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of each of the following propositions from Islamic sources: (a) 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (كل مسكر حرام). (b) 'Man is capable' (الإنسان ممكن). (c) 'Zayd is standing' (زيد قائم). (d) 'Some scholars are generous' (بعض العلماء كرماء). Explain your reasoning.
?
Write a brief defense of Ibn Sina's threefold division using the argument given in the passage. Why does excluding the natural proposition not break exhaustiveness?

The Unquantified Proposition: Definition and Key Features
Definition of the unquantified proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an unquantified proposition and distinguish it from quantified and natural propositions using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
An unquantified proposition judges individuals but hides whether all or some.

The unquantified proposition (muhmala) is a type of categorical proposition where the quantity (all or some) is omitted. Understanding it is crucial because many Quranic verses and legal statements are phrased in this way, and their interpretation depends on recognizing that quantity is not specified. In this lesson, we dissect its definition and see how it differs from other proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'muhmala' (unquantified). Note its Arabic root meaning 'neglected' – quantity is neglected.
2
Extract the genus
Determine the broader category. From the phrase 'the judgment in it is upon the individuals of its subject', we see it is a type of categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) because it ascribes a predicate to a subject.
The genus is 'categorical proposition'.
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes it from other categorical propositions? The definition says: 'the statement of its quantity, whether all or some, has been omitted.' So the differentia is the omission of the quantifier (all or some).
Compare with quantified propositions like 'Every human is mortal' (universal) or 'Some human is writer' (particular).
4
Contrast with the natural proposition
The unquantified proposition is often confused with the natural proposition (tabi'iyya). In the natural proposition, the judgment is about the nature itself, not about individuals. In the unquantified, the judgment is about individuals, but the quantity is unspecified. For example, 'Man is in loss' (Quran 103:2) is unquantified if interpreted as referring to individuals, but natural if interpreted as the essence of man.
This distinction is a critical refinement: the unquantified judges individuals, the natural judges the nature.

The Distributive Meaning of 'Kull' in Universal Propositions
Author clarifies that 'kull' in universal propositions means each individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between distributive and collective uses of 'kull' in universal propositions and identify which use is the logical quantifier.

Core Idea
'Kull' in logic means each individual, not the group.

In this passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya, the author clarifies a crucial point about the quantifier 'kull' (every) in universal affirmative propositions. Many students mistakenly think 'kull' refers to the whole group, but in logic it refers to each individual member. This distinction is essential for correct syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'kull'
The passage distinguishes between 'kull' as each individual (distributive) and 'kull' as collective whole (sum of individuals or parts).
2
Analyze the logical example
Look at 'Every human is an animal' – it means each individual human, not all humans together.
3
Contrast with non-logical examples
The passage gives examples where 'kull' means collective: 'Every human does not satisfy this loaf of bread' (collective), and 'I bought the whole slave' (sum of parts). In these, 'kull' is not a quantifier but a subject.
4
Apply the distinction
Check a universal proposition: Does it hold for each individual? If not, 'kull' might be collective and not a logical quantifier.

Distorted Propositions: Quantifiers on the Predicate
Explains the natural position of quantifiers and introduces distorted propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a distorted proposition (munḥarifa) and explain why placing the quantifier on the predicate violates the natural logical form.

Core Idea
Quantifiers belong on the subject because only the subject's scope is variable.

In classical logic, quantifiers like 'every' and 'some' indicate whether a predicate applies to all or part of the subject. The natural position of the quantifier is on the subject, because we need to know how many of the subject the predicate is true of. Placing the quantifier on the predicate distorts the proposition and is called a distorted proposition (munḥarifa). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule and shows how to recognize such distortions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural position of the quantifier
Read the passage carefully. The author states that quantifiers should properly enter upon the subject (al-mawḍū‘). Why? Because the subject is that of which the predicate is affirmed; it can be all or some individuals. The predicate does not have that variability.
2
Understand the reason for the distortion
If you place the quantifier on the predicate, the proposition deviates from its natural form. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (quantifier on subject) is natural, but 'Intoxicant is every forbidden thing' is distorted because it misrepresents the logical relationship.
The predicate is what is said about the subject; it doesn't naturally take a quantifier.
3
Learn the four types of distorted propositions
The logicians classified distorted propositions into four kinds: universal predicate with universal subject, universal predicate with particular subject, particular predicate with universal subject, and particular predicate with particular subject. The author mentions this classification but notes that further details are in advanced books.
These four arise from considering the predicate as quantified (either universal or particular) and the subject as either universal or particular.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take a valid proposition from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (كل مسكر حرام). The quantifier 'every' is on the subject. Now construct a distorted version: 'Intoxicant is every forbidden thing' (المسكر كل حرام). Notice how the quantifier now attaches to the predicate, making the statement ambiguous or false.
The distorted version could be misinterpreted as saying that intoxicants are all forbidden things, which is not true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اعلم أن اسوار المحصورات حقها ان تدخل على الموضوع لان الموضوع هو ما صدق عليه المحمول وما صدق عليه الشيء يحتمل ان يكون كل الافراد او بعضها فست الحاجة الى بيان ذلك بخلاف المحمول فانه الصادق على الموضوع والصادق على الشيء لا يجرى فيه ذلك الاحتمال فاذا أدخلت على المحمول فقد انحرفت القضية عن الوضع الطبيعي وتسمى ح منحرفة و حصروا اقسام المنحرفات في الاربعة قالوا: لان المحمول المسوراما ان يكون جزئياً أو كلياً وعليهما اما ان يكون الموضوع جزئياً أو كلياً، فهذه أربعة اقسام من ملاحظة الاثنين مع الاثنين وقد أطالوا فى تحقيق ذلك الكلام و ان شئت فعليك بالكتب المبسوطة المصنفة في هذا الفن.
And know that the quantifiers of quantified propositions should properly enter upon the subject, because the subject is that of which the predicate is affirmed, and that of which a thing is affirmed can be either all individuals or some of them, so there is need to clarify that. Unlike the predicate, for it is that which is affirmed of the subject, and that which is affirmed of something does not admit of that possibility. So if you place it on the predicate, the proposition has deviated from its natural form and is called a distorted proposition (munḥarifa). And they restricted the divisions of distorted propositions to four. They said: because the quantified predicate is either particular or universal, and in both cases the subject is either particular or universal. So these are four divisions by considering the two with the two. And they have elaborated extensively on investigating that discussion, and if you wish, then refer to the extensive books composed on this art.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage defines a key concept in propositional logic: quantifiers should naturally attach to the subject of a proposition. The subject is the entity about which we want to know whether the predicate applies to all or some, so the quantifier (like 'all', 'some', 'no') clarifies the quantity. The predicate, on the other hand, is what is asserted or denied of the subject; it does not vary in quantity relative to the subject. Therefore, if a quantifier is placed on the predicate, the proposition deviates from its natural order and is called 'munḥarifa' (distorted). For example, in fiqh, a statement like 'every intoxicant is forbidden' has the quantifier on the subject. If we say 'intoxicant is every forbidden thing', that would be distorted. The author notes that distorted propositions have been classified into four types: (1) universal predicate with universal subject, (2) universal predicate with particular subject, (3) particular predicate with universal subject, (4) particular predicate with particular subject. The passage ends by noting that logicians have written extensively on this topic and directs the reader to longer works for a full investigation. This concept is important for ensuring clarity in logical arguments, as misplacing quantifiers can lead to fallacies.

Key Terms
منحرفة
اسوار المحصورات
موضوع
محمول

Scope of Negation in 'ليس كل' and 'ليس بعض'
Author distinguishes two interpretations of 'ليس كل' and 'ليس بعض' based on scope of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scope-based interpretations of 'ليس كل' and 'ليس بعض' and correctly convert them into categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Scope of negation flips 'ليس كل' and 'ليس بعض' between particular and universal negatives.

This passage tackles a subtle ambiguity: does 'ليس كل' mean 'not every' (particular negative) or 'all not' (universal negative)? The answer depends on whether the quantifier is inside or outside the scope of negation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting negated statements in Islamic legal and theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases
Focus on the two key expressions: 'ليس كل' (not every) and 'ليس بعض' (not some). Note that they are ambiguous without scope specification.
2
Understand the baseline interpretation
According to the passage, if the negation applies to the entire proposition (quantifier inside scope), 'ليس كل' means 'رفع الايجاب الكلى' (removal of universal affirmative) i.e., particular negative (some A not B). 'ليس بعض' means 'رفع الايجاب الجزئى' (removal of particular affirmative) i.e., universal negative (no A is B).
This is the 'after negation' interpretation.
3
Understand the alternative interpretation
If the negation applies only to the predicate (quantifier outside scope), then 'ليس كل' becomes universal negative (no A is B), and 'ليس بعض' becomes particular negative (some A not B).
The passage calls this 'considering with respect to the predicate' or 'not delaying the quantifier after negation'.
4
Learn the rule of thumb
When 'كل' or 'بعض' are 'delayed' after negation (i.e., negation has wide scope), they have one meaning; when they are not delayed (negation has narrow scope), they have the opposite meaning.
Memorizing this rule helps in interpreting texts.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the statement 'ليس كل بيع حلال' (not every sale is lawful). If negation has wide scope, it means 'some sales are not lawful' (particular negative). If narrow scope, it means 'no sale is lawful' (universal negative). The correct interpretation depends on context.
This example from fiqh shows the practical importance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا محصل الفرق على ما هو المذكور فى كتب القوم واقول : ان الفرق المذكور ظاهر بالنسبة الى بعض «ليس و اما بالنسبة الى «ليس بعض فلا، فإن مفهومه الصريح رفع الايجاب الجزئى كما ان مفهوم ليس كل هو رفع الايجاب الكلى بناء على القاعدة المذكورة. والتحقيق ان يقال ان ليس كل» و «ليس بعض ان اعتبر سلبهما بالنسبة الى القضية التي بعد هما فمفهوم «ليس كل بالمطابقة هو رفع الايجاب الكلى السلب الجزئى و مفهوم «ليس بعض» بالمطابقة هو رفع الايجاب الجزئى و هو السلب الكلى وان اعتبر بالنسبة إلى المحمول فالمفهوم المطابق لـ «ليس كل» هو السلب الكلى و لـ «ليس بعض هو السلب الجزئى وبعبارة أخرى أوضح من ذلك، اذا اعتبر تأخر «بعض» و «كل» عن النفى فـ ليس كل لرفع الايجاب الكلى و ليس بعض» ترفع الايجاب الجزئى و اذا لم يعتبر تأخر هما عنه فى «ليس كل السلب الكلى و ليس بعض السلب الجزئى فتأمل.
This is the gist of the difference according to what is mentioned in the books of the people, and I say: The mentioned difference is apparent with respect to 'not every' but with respect to 'not some' it is not, for its explicit meaning is the removal of the particular affirmative just as the meaning of 'not every' is the removal of the universal affirmative according to the mentioned rule. And the investigation is to say: 'not every' and 'not some' — if their negation is considered with respect to the proposition that follows them, then the meaning of 'not every' by conformity is the removal of the universal affirmative (the particular negative), and the meaning of 'not some' by conformity is the removal of the particular affirmative (the universal negative). And if considered with respect to the predicate, then the meaning conforming to 'not every' is the universal negative, and to 'not some' is the particular negative. And in clearer words: if the delay of 'some' and 'every' after the negation is considered, then 'not every' is for the removal of the universal affirmative, and 'not some' removes the particular affirmative. And if their delay after it is not considered, then in 'not every' it is the universal negative, and in 'not some' it is the particular negative. So ponder.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the negated quantified propositions 'ليس كل' (not every) and 'ليس بعض' (not some). The author begins by noting that the standard distinction in logic books is clear for 'ليس كل' but not for 'ليس بعض'. He then provides a more precise investigation based on the scope of the negation. If we consider the negation as applying to the entire proposition that follows (i.e., the quantifier is inside the scope of negation), then 'ليس كل' means 'not (every A is B)', which is equivalent to 'some A is not B' (particular negative). Conversely, 'ليس بعض' means 'not (some A is B)', which is equivalent to 'no A is B' (universal negative). However, if we consider the quantifier as being outside the negation (i.e., the quantifier applies to the negated predicate), then 'ليس كل' means 'every A is not B' (universal negative), and 'ليس بعض' means 'some A is not B' (particular negative). The author summarizes this by saying: if we treat 'بعض' and 'كل' as delayed after the negation (i.e., within its scope), then 'ليس كل' is the removal of universal affirmative (particular negative), and 'ليس بعض' is removal of particular affirmative (universal negative). If we do not treat them as delayed, then 'ليس كل' is universal negative and 'ليس بعض' is particular negative. The passage ends with 'فتأمل' (ponder), indicating that the reader should reflect on this subtle distinction.

رفع الايجاب الكلى

Key Takeaway
The logical meaning of negated quantifiers 'ليس كل' and 'ليس بعض' depends entirely on whether the quantifier falls inside or outside the scope of negation; ambiguity is resolved by specifying scope.

Distinction between 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' and deviated propositions
Author distinguishes two negated quantifier forms and introduces deviated propositions with four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical forms 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' and identify the four types of deviated propositions (منحرفة) using the rules of quantifier placement.

Core Idea
The position of the negation particle changes the quantifier's force; attaching quantifiers to the predicate creates deviated propositions.

Islamic logic requires precise analysis of negated quantifiers: 'ليس بعض' (not some) and 'بعض ليس' (some not) have distinct ranges. Additionally, when quantifiers are attached to the predicate instead of the subject, the proposition is called 'منحرفة' (deviated). Understanding these forms is essential for interpreting arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two negated quantifier forms
Locate the phrases 'ليس بعض' (laysa ba'd) and 'بعض ليس' (ba'd laysa) in the Arabic. Note the placement of the negation particle 'ليس' relative to the quantifier 'بعض'.
In 'ليس بعض', negation precedes the quantifier; in 'بعض ليس', it follows.
2
Analyze 'ليس بعض' (not some)
This form can be interpreted as universal negative (equivalent to 'no') or particular negative depending on context. It can never be affirmative because the negation particle removes what follows.
Ambiguity resolved by the two considerations mentioned in prior lesson.
3
Analyze 'بعض ليس' (some not)
Normally indicates a particular negative (some are not). It is not universal negative because negation intervenes between subject and predicate. However, it can be used for affirmative if the negation is made part of the predicate (modified predicate).
The exception: when the predicate itself is negated, the proposition becomes affirmative (e.g., 'some are non-existent').
4
Understand deviated propositions (منحرفة)
Quantifiers naturally attach to the subject, which has the ambiguity of all/some. When a quantifier is attached to the predicate, the proposition deviates from the natural order and is called 'منحرفة'.
The predicate does not have the same ambiguity, so attaching a quantifier to it is unnatural.
5
Enumerate the four types of deviated propositions
The quantified predicate can be universal or particular, and the subject can be universal or particular. This yields four types: (1) universal subject - universal predicate, (2) universal subject - particular predicate, (3) particular subject - universal predicate, (4) particular subject - particular predicate.
The passage states these are from 'considering the two with the two'.

Source Text

And however it may be, the mentioned difference in their books is not correct. This is the difference between the first and the last. As for the difference between the first and the second: the first, namely 'not some', may be mentioned for universal negative and may be mentioned for particular negative according to the two mentioned considerations, and it is never mentioned for affirmative, because the particle of negation is placed to remove what follows it, so it is impossible to obtain an affirmative by it. The second, namely 'some not', is not mentioned for universal negative, because negation did not enter upon 'some' but rather 'some' is the subject, and when the negation particle intervenes between two things, it requires the negation of what follows it from what precedes it, so it requires here the negation of the predicate from the part, so it is not particular. And it may be mentioned for affirmative if the negation particle is made part of the predicate, according to the rule of the affirmative with a modified predicate, as will come. And know that the quantifiers of quantified propositions rightfully enter upon the subject, because the subject is that of which the predicate is true, and that of which a thing is true may be all individuals or some of them, so there is need to clarify that, unlike the predicate, for it is that which is true of the subject, and that which is true of a thing does not admit that possibility. So if they are entered upon the predicate, the proposition deviates from the natural position and is called deviated (munharifa). They enumerated the types of deviated propositions as four. They said: because the quantified predicate is either particular or universal, and in each case the subject is either particular or universal, so these are four types from considering the two with the two. And they have elaborated on investigating that discussion, and if you wish, consult the extensive books composed on this art.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two important logical forms: 'ليس بعض' (not some) and 'بعض ليس' (some not). The author begins by stating that the earlier distinction in the books is not correct, and then provides the proper distinction. He refers to 'الأول' (the first) as 'ليس بعض' and 'الثاني' (the second) as 'بعض ليس'. For 'ليس بعض', it can be used for either universal negative or particular negative depending on the interpretation (as discussed in the previous lesson), but it is never used for affirmative because the negation particle removes what follows. For 'بعض ليس', it is not used for universal negative because the negation does not apply to 'some' itself; rather, 'بعض' is the subject and the negation particle intervenes, requiring the negation of the predicate from that part, resulting in a particular negative. However, 'بعض ليس' can be used for affirmative if the negation is made part of the predicate (i.e., the predicate is a negated term, making the overall proposition affirmative). The passage then shifts to a general discussion of quantifiers (اسوار المحصورات). It states that quantifiers properly enter upon the subject because the subject is what the predicate is attributed to, and it may be all or some individuals. The predicate, being what is attributed, does not have that ambiguity. Therefore, if a quantifier is attached to the predicate, the proposition becomes 'منحرفة' (deviated) from the natural order. The deviated propositions are classified into four types based on whether the quantified predicate is particular or universal and whether the subject is particular or universal. The author notes that this topic is elaborated in longer works.

Key Terms
ليس بعض
بعض ليس
منحرفة
اسوار المحصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Both universal and particular negative

Key Takeaway
The placement of negation relative to the quantifier determines the logical force, and deviated propositions arise when quantifiers modify the predicate instead of the subject.

Equivalence of Important and Particular Propositions
Commentary explains why important propositions are subsumed under particular due to equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the equivalence between the important (muhmala) and particular (juz'iyya) propositions and explain why logicians consider the important redundant.

Core Idea
The important proposition is equivalent to the particular; thus, the particular suffices.

This passage from the commentary explains a key logical insight: the proposition without a quantifier (al-muhmala) and the quantified particular (al-juz'iyya) are equivalent in truth conditions. Understanding this equivalence simplifies our classification of propositions—only quantified ones are needed. Let's walk through the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Recall that an important proposition (مهملة) has no quantifier, e.g., 'الإنسان في خسر' (Man is in loss). A particular proposition (جزئية) uses 'بعض' (some), e.g., 'بعض الإنسان في خسر' (Some men are in loss).
2
Understand the truth condition of the important
For the important to be true, the judgment must apply to the individuals of the subject in general—either all or some. The passage says: 'المعتبر في المهملة ان يصدق الحكم على افراد الموضوع فى الجملة'—the considered in the important is that the judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general.
3
Show forward direction: important implies particular
If the important is true, then the judgment holds for the subject's individuals in general (in some sense). In both cases (all or some), the particular is true because it only requires some individuals. The passage: 'على كلا التقديرين تصدق الجزئية'.
4
Show reverse direction: particular implies important
If a particular proposition (some individuals are in loss) is true, then the important proposition is also true because the general statement (man is in loss) covers that some. The passage says: 'اذا صدق الحكم على بعض الافراد ... صدق على الافراد في الجملة'.
5
Conclude the redundancy
Since the truth conditions are identical, the important proposition is subsumed under the particular. Logicians only count quantified propositions (universal and particular) as independent types. The passage: 'فلم يعتبروا المهملة لاغناء الجزئية عنها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۶) قوله لان المهملة و الجزئية متلازمتان»: لما قسم القضية فيها تقدم الى الاربعة وقال هيهنا ان المعتبرة منها هي المحصورات، تصدى الى بيان الانحصار وقال: «لان المهملة والجزئية متلازمتان انه كلما صدقت المهملة صدقت الجزئية وبالعكس. اما الاول فلان المعتبر في المهملة ان يصدق الحكم على افراد الموضوع فى الجملة سواء كان على جميع الافراد او على بعضها وعلى كلا التقديرين تصدق الجزئية لان الحكم فيها على بعض الافراد مطلقا اي مع السكوت عن البعض الآخر كما تقدم. و اما الثاني فلظهور انه اذا صدق الحكم على بعض الافراد بالمعنى المذكور صدق على الافراد في الجملة فظهر ان المهملة مندرجة تحت الجزئية فلم يعتبروا المهملة لاغناء الجزئية عنها. (ميز را محمد على) (۳۷) قوله اذ كلما صدق الحكم على افراد الموضوع في الجملة»: كما في موضوع المهملة، فان قولنا: الانسان في خسر يلزمه ان افراد الانسان على نحو الاجمال محكومة بالخسران، فحتماً هذا العنوان الاجمالي يلزمه ان يصدق مع الحكم بالخسران على بعض الافراد بالقطع، والأكذبت القضية، و كلما صدق الحكم على بعض الافراد بالقطع صدق على الافراد بالاجمال، لأن الاجمال لما تصادق مع البعض
(36) His statement: 'Because the important (muhmala) and the particular (juz'iyya) are equivalent': When he divided the proposition earlier into four and said here that the considered among them are the quantified, he undertook to explain the limitation and said: 'Because the important and the particular are equivalent: whenever the important is true, the particular is true, and vice versa. As for the first, because what is considered in the important is that the judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general, whether it is of all individuals or of some. In both cases the particular is true, because the judgment in it is of some individuals absolutely, i.e., with silence about the others, as previously. As for the second, because it is apparent that when the judgment is true of some individuals in the mentioned sense, it is true of the individuals in general. So it appears that the important is subsumed under the particular, so they did not consider the important because the particular suffices for it. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (37) His statement: 'Because whenever the judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general': as in the subject of the important, for our saying 'Man is in loss' necessitates that the individuals of man are in loss in a general way. So necessarily this general title necessitates that it is true with the judgment of loss of some individuals with certainty, otherwise the proposition would be false. And whenever the judgment is true of some individuals with certainty, it is true of the individuals in general, because the general is equivalent to the particular.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the statement that the important (المهملة) and particular (الجزئية) propositions are equivalent (متلازمتان). The commentator (likely Mirza Muhammad Ali) explains why this equivalence holds. First, if an important proposition (e.g., 'الإنسان في خسر' - Man is in loss, without a quantifier) is true, it means the judgment applies to the individuals of the subject in general, whether to all or to some. In either case, the particular proposition (e.g., 'بعض الإنسان في خسر' - Some man is in loss) is true because the particular affirms the judgment for some individuals without specifying the rest. Conversely, if a particular proposition is true (some individuals are in loss), then the important proposition is also true because the judgment applies to the individuals in general (since 'some' implies 'in general'). Thus, the important proposition is subsumed under the particular, and logicians do not consider the important as a separate type because the particular suffices. The passage further illustrates this with an example: if we say 'Man is in loss', it necessarily implies that some individuals are definitely in loss; otherwise the statement would be false. And if some are definitely in loss, then the general statement is true because the general covers that some.

Key Terms
المهملة (al-muhmala): The important proposition: a proposition without a quantifier, e.g., 'الإنسان في خسر' (Man is in loss). [Note: No significant shift.]
الجزئية (al-juz'iyya): The particular proposition: a proposition with the quantifier 'بعض' (some), e.g., 'بعض الإنسان في خسر' (Some man is in loss). [Note: No significant shift.]
متلازمتان (mutalāzimatān): Equivalent in truth conditions: each implies the other. [Note: This term is used in logic for mutual implication.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are always false
B) They are equivalent to universal propositions
C) They are equivalent to particular propositions
D) They cannot be used in syllogisms

Key Takeaway
The important proposition is not an independent logical type; it is equivalent to the particular, so all unquantified propositions are treated as particular in logical analysis.

Why Singular and Natural Propositions Are Excluded from Sciences
Author explains exclusion of singular and natural propositions from sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why singular and natural propositions are unsuitable for scientific inquiry and identify the four quantified propositions that are required.

Core Idea
Sciences need general rules; singular and natural propositions lack quantification.

In this passage, the author explains a key methodological principle: only propositions that can yield general rules are fit for scientific discourse. Singular propositions refer to specific individuals, while natural propositions consider essences stripped of individuals. Both fail to provide the universal applicability that sciences demand. Understanding this will help you grasp why logic restricts itself to quantified propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two excluded proposition types
The author names two: singular (شخصية) and natural (طبيعية). Locate them in the Arabic.
Natural is also called 'real' (حقيقية).
2
Understand exclusion of singular propositions
Sciences need propositions that yield general rules and universal criteria. Singular propositions are tied to specific individuals and do not generalize.
Example: 'Zayd is standing' tells us nothing about anyone else.
3
Understand exclusion of natural propositions
Natural propositions consider the essence itself, stripped of its individuals. They lack quantification over individuals, which sciences need.
Example: 'Human is rational' does not specify quantity.
4
Recognize required proposition type
The author states that sciences need propositions with subjects where the quantity of individuals is clarified. This only occurs in the four quantified propositions.
These are المحصورات أربع (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative).

Commentary
This passage gives the epistemological reason for restricting logical analysis to quantified propositions. Sciences aim to establish general rules and universal principles. Singular propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') are tied to specific individuals and cannot support general knowledge. Natural propositions (e.g., 'Human is a species') consider the essence stripped of individuals, so they lack quantification over individuals. Therefore, only the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, etc.) are suitable for scientific discourse. The passage also excludes indefinite propositions (muhmala) when left indefinite, and the 'real' (haqiqiyya) propositions that are essentially natural.

Propositions Fit for Science: Excluding Personal and Natural
Author classifies proposition types and excludes personal and natural from scientific inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between personal, natural, and quantified propositions and explain why only quantified propositions are suitable for scientific inquiry.

Core Idea
Sciences need general laws; personal and natural propositions don't provide them.

In this passage, the author explains which types of propositions are appropriate for scientific investigation. Sciences rely on general rules that apply to many cases. Personal propositions about specific individuals and natural propositions that consider only the essence without individuals cannot serve this purpose. Understanding this classification helps you see why logic prioritizes quantified propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proposition types
The passage discusses personal, natural, and quantified propositions. Personal propositions refer to specific individuals (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). Natural propositions consider the nature itself abstracted from individuals (e.g., 'Man is an animal' considering humanity in itself). Quantified propositions use universal or particular quantifiers (e.g., 'Every human is mortal').
The indefinite proposition is mentioned as subsumed under the particular.
2
Understand the scientific criterion
The author states that sciences need propositions that can be encompassed by general rules and beneficial universal criteria. In other words, a proposition in a science must yield a general principle that applies across multiple instances.
This criterion is key to excluding certain proposition types.
3
Apply the criterion to personal propositions
Personal propositions are about a single individual. Their truth does not provide a general rule; they are 'foreign' to the idea of a general benefit. Therefore, they are not researched in the sciences.
Example: 'Zayd is a scholar' is not useful for establishing a general rule about scholars.
4
Apply the criterion to natural propositions
Natural propositions look at the nature itself without considering individuals. They also fail because sciences need subjects with determined quantity of individuals. The natural proposition abstracts away from quantity, so it cannot be used to formulate general or particular rules.
The passage explicitly says these propositions are not researched in the sciences.
5
Verify why quantified propositions succeed
The four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) have subjects with determined quantity. This allows them to express rules that are general or at least particular, meeting the scientific need.
These are the main tools for scientific reasoning.

Source Text

This passage defines the types of propositions that are fit for scientific inquiry. It begins by stating that the indefinite proposition is included under the particular because its certain content is particular, while the extra doubtful part is ignored. The author then rules out personal propositions because sciences require general rules and criteria, which personal propositions lack. Similarly, the natural proposition, which considers external nature stripped of individuals, is excluded because sciences need to formulate universal or at least particular rules. Such rules require subjects with determined quantity of individuals, which only occurs in the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). Thus, personal, indefinite, and natural propositions are excluded from what sciences need.

Objection and answer on immaterial particulars
Objector raises immaterial particulars; answer says they become universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the distinction between particular and universal aspects resolves an objection about immaterial particulars in scientific study.

Core Idea
Immaterial particulars become universal when studied in sciences.

The earlier discussion established that particulars are not researched as particulars in sciences because they change. An objector points out that immaterial particulars (like angels) do not change, so they might be an exception. The commentator resolves this by explaining that even immaterial particulars are studied under universal concepts, not as individual particulars. Thus, the original claim remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objector's statement: immaterial particulars (al-juz'iyyat al-mujarrada) do not change, so they can provide perfection as particulars.
2
Locate the answer
The commentator answers: 'الجزئيات بهذا الاعتبار تصير كلية أيضاً' – particulars under this consideration become universal as well.
3
Understand the distinction
The key move: research in sciences is always on universal aspects ('على وجه كلى'), not on the particular itself. Even for immaterial particulars, we study them via universal concepts.
This resolves the objection because it shows no contradiction: immaterial particulars are not studied as particulars but as universals.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Think of how 'angel' (e.g., Jibril) is defined in Kalam using universal categories like 'spirit' or 'intellect' – this is studying a particular under a universal aspect.

The Natural Proposition vs. Quantified Propositions
Clarifying that natural proposition is not included among the four quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the natural proposition from the four quantified propositions and explain why the natural proposition is not included among them.

Core Idea
The natural proposition judges the nature itself, not individuals, so it stands outside the quantified four.

In the study of propositions (qadaya), logicians classify propositions in various ways. One key distinction is between quantified propositions (al-mahsurat) and the natural proposition (al-tabi'iyya). The quantified propositions—universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative—make judgments about individuals or sets of individuals. The natural proposition, however, makes a judgment about the nature (tabi'a) itself. This lesson clarifies why the natural proposition is not to be counted among the four quantified types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage mentions 'the enumerated [quantified propositions]' (al-mahsurat) and 'the natural' (al-tabi'iyya). Recognize that these are two distinct categories of propositions.
2
Understand the subject of quantified propositions
Quantified propositions have judgments 'about individuals and persons' (ala al-afrad wa al-ashkhas). That is, their subjects refer to individual members or all/some individuals of a class.
For example, 'Every human is mortal' judges each individual human.
3
Understand the subject of the natural proposition
The natural proposition is not like that. Its subject is the nature itself (al-tabi'a) as such, not the individuals that instantiate it.
For example, 'Human is a rational animal' predicates a property of the nature 'human'.
4
Apply the distinction
Given this difference, determine why the natural proposition cannot be reduced to any of the four quantified forms. The answer is that the quantified forms always involve individuals, while the natural proposition does not.
This is why the passage states 'the natural is not like that' (wa al-tabi'iyya laysat kadhalik).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ای لا بخصوصها كما ذكر ولا في ضمن المحصورات فان الحكم في المحصورات على الافراد و الاشخاص والطبيعية ليست كذلك (ميرزا محمد علی)

The reason is that quantified propositions have judgments about individuals and persons (i.e., their subjects are individuals or sets of individuals), whereas the natural proposition's subject is the nature itself, not individuals.

Correcting the Commentator on Natural Propositions
Correcting a commentator's mistake about natural proposition subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct understanding of subject existence in affirmative and negative propositions and explain the commentator's error.

Core Idea
A proposition's subject must exist for the predicate to attach, except in negative propositions.

In this passage, the author corrects a commentator who misdefined the subject of a natural proposition. He then reiterates a fundamental logical principle: the predicate requires an existing subject. This principle is crucial for distinguishing valid from invalid propositions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the error
The commentator defined the subject of the natural proposition as either the concept applicable to many or the external reality stripped of particularities. Find this mistake in the first line of the passage.
2
Understand the correction
The author states 'هذا اشتباه من الشارح' and clarifies what the subject is not. Recognize that the author does not give a positive definition here but refutes the commentator's definition.
3
Extract the principle for affirmatives
In an affirmative proposition, the subject must exist (externally or mentally) because the predicate needs a locus to attach to. If the locus has no existence, the predicate cannot apply.
This is why 'The phoenix exists' is false or invalid: the subject does not exist.
4
Apply the exception for negatives
Negative propositions can be true even if the subject does not exist. The text mentions 'saaliba bi-intifaa' al-mawdu'' as a type of negative where the subject is verbally mentioned but non-existent.
Example: 'The phoenix does not exist' is true even though 'phoenix' has no external reality.
5
Relate to the concept of 'sa'iba'
The passage also discusses 'al-sa'iba' (the convertible proposition), which requires an existing subject to 'release' something from it. Understand that this is another type requiring subject existence.
The exact nature of 'sa'iba' needs further verification, but the principle applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « كما هو موضوع الطبيعية»: هذا اشتباه من الشارح، فان موضوع الطبيعية ليس هو المفهوم الجائز الصدق على كثيرين و لما هو الحقيقة الخارجية الموجودة في الخارج محذوفاً عنها خصوصيات و تعینات افرادها الجزئية المتشتتة كما انبئناك عن خبر هذا قبل حين قوله « ولابد في الموجبة من وجود الموضوع والا امتنعت القضية عن التركيب رأساً، لان المحمول لابد له من محط يرد عليه، فاذا كان المحط لا وجود له امتنع المحمول نفسه بالضرورة و السائبة كذلك لابد لها من موضوع موجود حتى يفك عنه ما يراد فكه عنه، سوى ان السالبة قد تصدق و الموضوع منتف وجوداً مذكور لفظاً وهي التي يقال لها سالبة بانتفاء الموضوع. (التقريب ص ٤٣)
His statement: 'As is the subject of the natural [proposition]': This is a mistake of the commentator. For the subject of the natural [proposition] is not the concept that is applicable to many, nor is it the external reality existing in the outside stripped of the particularities and determinations of its scattered individual instances, as we informed you of this earlier when he said: 'And in the affirmative it is necessary to have existence of the subject, otherwise the proposition would be prevented from composition entirely. Because the predicate must have a locus to attach to; if the locus has no existence, the predicate itself is necessarily impossible. And the convertible [proposition] also must have an existing subject so that what is intended to be released from it is released, except that the negative may be true while the subject is non-existent in existence but mentioned verbally, and it is called a negative by the nonexistence of the subject. (al-Taqrib p.43)'
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage corrects a mistake by a previous commentator (al-Sharih). The commentator had incorrectly defined the subject of the natural proposition as either the concept predictable of many or the external reality stripped of particularities. The author refutes this, stating that the subject is not that. He then reiterates a fundamental logical principle: in an affirmative proposition, the subject must exist (either externally or mentally) because the predicate needs a locus. If the locus does not exist, the predicate cannot apply. This is contrasted with negative propositions, which can be true even if the subject does not exist (e.g., 'The phoenix does not exist').

Key Terms
السائبة
سالبة بانتفاء الموضوع

Key Takeaway
In affirmative propositions, the subject must exist for the predicate to attach; negative propositions can be true even if the subject does not exist.

Existence of the Natural Universal Depends on Position
Comment on the existence of natural universal depending on philosophical position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two main positions on the existence of natural universals and explain how each position affects the treatment of natural propositions.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists externally only if you are a realist — otherwise, it does not exist at all.

This short comment by Muhammad Ali clarifies a crucial philosophical divide that underlies discussions of natural propositions. The existence of the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) in the external world is not universally accepted; it depends on one's ontological commitments. Understanding this divide is essential for correctly interpreting logical propositions that involve natural kinds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
From the passage, note the two contrasting views: one holds that the natural universal exists externally through its individuals (realism), while the other holds that universal natures do not exist at all in the external world (nominalism or conceptualism).
The phrase 'هذا على القول بوجود الكلى الطبيعي في الخارج' indicates the first position; 'على القول الآخر' indicates the second.
2
Understand the implication for logic
On the realist view, propositions about natural kinds (e.g., 'Man is rational') refer to real extramental entities. On the nominalist view, such propositions are merely mental constructs or linguistic conventions, requiring a different logical treatment.
The commentator says that on the other position, the discussion of natural propositions would need emendation (استدراك).
3
Recognize the need for position-awareness
The passage signals that one cannot assume the existence of natural universals without adopting a specific philosophical stance. In logical analysis, one must first establish which ontological framework is in play.
This is a meta-logical point: the validity or interpretation of arguments involving natural kinds depends on prior philosophical commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا على القول بوجود الكلى الطبيعي في الخارج بوجود افراده واما على القول الآخر فلا يخفى استدراكه فان الطبايع الكلية غير موجودة عندار باب هذا القول مطلقا . (محمد علي)
This is according to the position that the natural universal exists in the external world through the existence of its individuals. But according to the other position, its emendation is not hidden, for the universal natures are not existent at all in the door of this position. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This short note clarifies that the previous discussion about natural propositions depends on which philosophical position one adopts regarding the existence of natural universals. One view (realism) holds that natural universals exist in the external world through their individuals. The other view (nominalism or conceptualism) holds that universal natures do not exist externally at all. The commentator implies that on the latter view, the treatment of natural propositions would require adjustment.

Key Terms
الكلى الطبيعي (al-kulli al-tabi'i): The natural universal, a universal by nature that exists in the external world through its individuals according to one view. [Note: Debated in Islamic philosophy; shifts between realism and nominalism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The existence of the natural universal is a philosophical assumption, not a given; logical analysis must account for which position is adopted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Sciences Exclude Natural Propositions
Brief proof that natural propositions are not studied in sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that excludes natural propositions from scientific inquiry and identify the middle term.

Core Idea
Natural propositions abstract from existence; sciences study existents, so they are excluded.

This brief proof from a hashia explains a key principle: sciences aim to know the states of actual existents, but natural propositions consider natures in themselves, abstracted from existence. Let's unpack the logic to see how this exclusion follows necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two premises
The proof states: (1) The purpose of the sciences is knowledge of the states of existent entities. (2) The natural proposition is not like that (i.e., not about existent entities). Write these as categorical statements.
Premise 1: 'All sciences are about existent entities.' Premise 2: 'No natural proposition is about existent entities.'
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term connects the two premises. Here, it is 'existent entities' (الموجودات المتاصلة). It appears in both premises.
Middle term: 'existent entities'.
3
Formulate the conclusion
From the premises, the conclusion is: 'No natural proposition is researched in the sciences.' This follows because if a subject does not fall under the scope of sciences (existent entities), it is not investigated.
Conclusion: 'Natural propositions are not researched in sciences.'
4
Check validity and figure
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure (Celarent mood). The middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. Check that the terms are distributed correctly.
Major: All sciences (S) are about existent entities (M). Minor: No natural proposition (P) is about existent entities (M). Conclusion: No natural proposition (P) is a science (S). Valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خبر لقوله : «فان»، وحاصل الدليل: ان المقصود من العلوم هو معرفة احوال الموجودات المتاصلة و الطبيعية ليست كذلك فلا يبحث عنها فيها . (عبد الرح)
It is the predicate of his statement: 'for'. The summary of the proof: the purpose of the sciences is knowledge of the states of existent entities, and the natural [proposition] is not like that, so it is not researched in them. (Abd al-Rahman)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This note supplies the predicate (خبر) for a previous 'inna' or 'anna' clause. It summarizes the proof that sciences aim to know the states of existent realities (موجودات متأصلة). Since natural propositions consider nature in itself, abstracted from existence, they do not pertain to existent entities; hence they are not researched in sciences.

Why Natural Propositions are Excluded from Science
Explanation of why natural propositions are excluded from scientific inquiry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why natural propositions are not suitable for scientific inquiry and identify the type of proposition used in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Nature qua nature has no external existence, so studying it yields no perfection in knowledge.

In this lesson, we examine a key distinction in propositional logic: the natural proposition judges nature in itself, which does not exist in the external world. This is crucial for understanding why the sciences (like Kalam, Fiqh) focus on propositions about existing realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the natural proposition
The subject is nature considered in itself (الطبيعة من حيث هي هي), abstracted from all individual instances and existence. This is a purely conceptual entity.
Contrast with existential propositions where the subject is a concrete individual or class.
2
Recognize its mode of existence
This nature in itself has no external existence; it exists only in the mind as a universal concept. The passage explicitly states that it is 'non-existent in the external world' (غير موجودة في الخارج).
3
Connect to the purpose of sciences
The sciences (such as Kalam and Fiqh) aim to know the states of entities that are well-established in existence (الموجودات المتأصلة في الوجود). The goal is knowledge of real, existing things and their properties.
For example, Kalam studies the existence of the world as a contingent entity requiring a creator.
4
Conclude why natural propositions are excluded
Since the subject (nature qua nature) does not exist externally, studying its states does not contribute to scientific knowledge. No perfection (كمال) is attained from such study, so natural propositions are not used in the sciences.
This justifies why logicians and theologians prioritize existential and conditional propositions over natural ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ای اذا تبين ان الحكم في الطبيعية على الطبيعة من حيث هي هي و هي غير موجودة في الخارج فلا كمال فى معرفة احوال الطبايع الغير الموجودة في الخارج حتى يبحث عن الطبيعية فان المقصود في العلوم معرفة احوال الموجودات المتأصلة في الوجود. (محمد على)
i.e., when it is clear that the judgment in the natural [proposition] is about nature qua nature, and it does not exist in the external world, then there is no perfection in knowing the states of natures that do not exist in the external world such that one would research the natural [proposition]. For the purpose of the sciences is knowledge of the states of entities that are well-established in existence. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This note elaborates on why natural propositions are excluded. The natural proposition judges nature considered in itself (من حيث هي هي), which has no external existence (as a separate entity). Since sciences aim to know the states of existent things (موجودات متأصلة), studying non-existent natures yields no perfection and is therefore not part of scientific inquiry.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
Natural propositions are excluded from sciences because their subject (nature in itself) does not exist externally, while sciences aim to know the states of existing realities.

The Principle of Attribution: Existence Prerequisite
Discussion of the principle that establishment of something for something is a branch of the establishment of the latter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle 'thubut shay' li shay' far' li thubut al-muthbat lahu' to both subject and predicate, and identify cases where the subject is mentally existent.

Core Idea
For a thing to be attributed to another, the recipient must first be established in itself.

This lesson unpacks a fundamental logical principle: the attribution of a property to something depends on that thing's own existence. You'll see how this applies to both subject and predicate, and how logicians handle cases of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Locate the quoted statement: 'The establishment of a thing for a thing is a branch of the establishment of the thing for which it is established.' This is the principle under discussion.
2
Apply to the Predicate (Second Thing)
The principle initially applies to the second thing (the predicate): for a predicate to be attributed to a subject, the predicate must be established in itself (e.g., 'whiteness' must exist to be attributed to an object).
3
Extend to the Subject (First Thing)
The commentator extends the principle: the subject too must be established in itself. A thing that does not exist in itself cannot receive an attribute. For example, a non-existent cannot be described as 'known'.
4
Understand 'Establishment' as General
The term 'establishment' includes both external and mental existence. Even mental images are considered established in the mind, allowing attribution to non-existent objects in a qualified sense.
This resolves issues with negative propositions or fictional entities.
5
Test with Problematic Examples
Consider the example 'nothing equals the impossible in general possibility'. The terms are mental images, not real existents. The principle still holds because the images are established in the mind. Recognize that attribution to non-existents is only possible via mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وثبوت شيء لشيء فرع لثبوت المثبت له : اى الشيء الثاني. :اقول هكذا الحال فى الشيء الأول، فان ثبوت شيء لغيره فرع ثبوت ذلك الشيء في نفسه، لان الشيء مالم يثبت في نفسه لم يثبت لغيره، والمراد بالثبوت المعنى الاعم كما سيجيء تفصيله انشاء الله تعالى بعيد هذا فلايرد نمود اللاشيء يساوى اللاممكن بالامكان العام» لأن الاعلام صور ذهنية. لا يقال : اثبات الصور لها فرع ذى الصور فلزم خلاف ما كنا فيه . محققاً لا نا نقول : ليس تلك الصور صوراً لتلك المعدومات حقيقة بل هى صور تحصل في الاذهان عقيب تخييل المعدومات للتفهيم والتفهم والاضافة اليها بادنى الملابسة فانما يصدق هذا الحكم اذا كان الموضوع موجوداً يتوجه عليه ان المتأخرين صرحوابان سالبة المحمول لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع مع انها موجبة
His statement: 'The establishment of a thing for a thing is a branch of the establishment of the thing for which it is established': i.e., the second thing. I say: So is the case for the first thing. For the establishment of a thing for another is a branch of the establishment of that thing in itself, because a thing, as long as it is not established in itself, is not established for another. And by 'establishment' is meant the general meaning, as its detail will come later, God willing, after this. So the example 'nothing equals the impossible in general possibility' does not apply, because the [negative] terms are mental images. It cannot be said: the establishment of images for them is a branch of the possessors of images, which would contradict what we are in. Truly we say: those images are not images of those non-existents in reality, but rather they are images that occur in the minds after imagining non-existents for teaching and understanding, and attribution to them with the slightest connection. So this judgment is true only if the subject exists. It is objected that the later scholars explicitly stated that the negative of the predicate does not require existence of the subject, while it is affirmative.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical principle 'thubut shay' li shay' far' li thubut al-muthbat lahu' (establishment of a thing for a thing is a branch of the establishment of that for which it is established). The commentator extends it to the first thing as well: a thing must be established in itself before it can be established for another. 'Establishment' here is general (including mental existence). An example about 'nothing equals the impossible' is dismissed because the terms are mental images, not real. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: later logicians say that a negative of the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing') does not require the subject to exist, yet such a proposition is affirmative in form. The passage ends mid-argument, possibly noting a conflict.

Key Terms
ثبوت شيء لشيء
المثبت له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It does not need to be established in itself.
B) It must be established in itself before being established for another.
C) It only needs to be established in the mind.
D) It is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The principle that attribution requires the existence of the recipient applies to both subject and predicate, with mental existence considered a valid form of establishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
