How Mental Predication Applies to the External World
Author refutes an objection about how mental relations can be in the external world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a mental relation and its existence, and explain how the external world can be a locus for the relation itself without it being an external entity.

Core Idea
A mental predication refers to the external world without itself existing there.

The objection is sharp: if predication (haml) and relation (nisba) are mere mental considerations, how can we say they 'occur' in the external world? The author’s answer hinges on a subtle but crucial distinction – the difference between the thing itself and its existence. Understanding this protects logical discourse from reifying mental entities, a trap common in both Kalam and Fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector asserts that relation and predication are purely mental (i'tibariyya), not external (kharijiyya). Therefore, the external world cannot be their locus (zarf).
2
Locate the author's key distinction
The author responds by distinguishing between something being a locus for the existence of a thing versus being a locus for the thing itself. An external entity is one whose existence has the external world as its locus. But a mental consideration can have the external world as a locus for itself (not for its existence).
For example, Zayd's existence is in the external world, so Zayd is external. But for a mental relation, the external world is not a locus for its existence (it has no existence), but a locus for the relation itself (the relation is about the external world).
3
Apply the distinction to the example
The passage uses the statement 'Zayd exists in the external world'. Here, Zayd is external because the external world contains his existences. In contrast, the relation (nisba) and predication (haml) are themselves contained in the external world as loci—not their existence.
Think of a mirror reflecting an object. The mirror is locus for the image, not for the object's existence.
4
Compare the two statements from Sharh al-Talkhis
The author cites: 'Standing occurs for Zayd in the external world' vs. 'The occurrence of standing for Zayd is an external reality'. The first is a mental predication correctly referring to an external state; the second falsely reifies the occurrence. Understanding why the second is false solidifies the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان قلت ان النسبة و الحمل من الامور الاعتبارية دون الخارجية، فكيف يصح ال ان يكون الخارج ظرفاً للنسبة والحمل ؟ قلت: لا منافاة فى كونهما من الامور الاعتبارية ووقوعه ظرفاً لهما ولا يلزم من ذلك كونها من الأمور الخارجية كما هو ظاهر فان الامر الخارجى ما كان الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده لاما كان ظرفاً لنفسه، مثلاً اذا قلنا: زيد موجود في الخارج يكون زيد امراً خارجياً لكون الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده لا وجوده لكون الخارج ظرفاً لنفسه ولاريب ان الخارج فيما نحن فيه ظرف لنفس النسبة والحمل لا لوجودهما فتأمل. و الى ذلك اشار المصنف فى شرح التلخيص تبعاً لشارح المطائع حيث قال: ولا يقدح في ذلك ان النسبة من الاعتبارية دون الخارجية للفرق الظاهر بين قولنا : القيام حاصل لزيد فى الخارج وحصول القيام له امر متحقق موجود في الخارج فلا يلزم من بطلان الثاني بطلان الاول
If you say: The relation and predication are among mental considerations, not external ones, so how is it correct that the external world is a locus for the relation and predication? I say: There is no contradiction in them being mental considerations and the external world being a locus for them, and it does not necessitate that they are external things, as is apparent. For an external thing is that whose existence has the external world as its locus, not that the external world is a locus for itself. For example, when we say: Zayd exists in the external world, Zayd is an external thing because the external world is a locus for his existence, not for his existence because the external world is a locus for itself. And there is no doubt that the external world in our case is a locus for the relation and predication themselves, not for their existence, so reflect. And the author pointed to this in his commentary on al-Talkhīṣ, following the commentator of al-Maṭāli‘, where he said: That the relation is among mental considerations, not external ones, does not harm this, because of the apparent difference between our saying: Standing occurs for Zayd in the external world, and the occurrence of standing for him is a realized thing existing in the external world. So the falsity of the second does not necessitate the falsity of the first.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection and answer. The objector argues: since relation (nisba) and predication (haml) are mental considerations, how can they be located in the external world? The author responds by distinguishing between an external thing itself and its existence. An external thing (like Zayd) is something whose existence is in the external world. But the external world can be a locus for the relation itself, not its existence. The author gives an example: 'Zayd exists in the external world' makes Zayd external because the external world contains his existence. In contrast, the external world contains the relation and predication themselves, not their existence. He supports this by citing the author of Sharh al-Talkhis, who says that the mental nature of relation does not invalidate it being in the external world, because 'standing occurs for Zayd in the external world' is different from 'the occurrence of standing for Zayd is an external reality'. The first is a predication in the mind that refers to an external state, while the second reifies the occurrence as an extra-mental entity. The former is valid, the latter is not. Thus, no contradiction arises.

Key Takeaway
The logical distinction between a thing and its existence allows mental predications to be about the external world without being external entities—a cornerstone of Islamic logic and theology.

Refuting the Conditional Compound Misconception
Author refutes a misconception that 'كلما لو وجد' forms a conditional compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct syntactic parsing of 'كلما لو وجد' in universal propositions and explain why it is not a conditional compound.

Core Idea
Every time is not a conditional tool; it's two words: every + relative pronoun.

Some logicians mistakenly treat the phrase 'كلما لو وجد' as a compound conditional, like 'if the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' The author refutes this by showing that 'كلما' is actually 'كل' (every) and 'ما' (relative pronoun), forming a subject-relative clause structure, not a conditional. Understanding this parsing is crucial for correctly interpreting universal propositions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Misconception
Find the claim that some people think 'كلما لو وجد في الخارج كان إنسانا فهو بحيث لو وجد كان حيوانا' is a conditional compound. Note how they compare it to a conditional like 'if the sun rises then daytime exists.'
2
Parse 'كلما' as Two Words
Break down 'كلما' into 'كل' (noun meaning 'every') and 'ما' (relative pronoun or described noun). The phrase 'ما لو وجد...' is a relative clause describing what is meant by 'كل'.
In common usage, 'كلما' often means 'whenever', but here it is analyzed as separate components.
3
Recognize the Subject-Predicate Structure
The sentence structure is: 'كل' (subject) + relative clause + predicate (فهو على تقدير وجوده حيوان). The 'fa' in the predicate indicates a conditional implication, but the whole is not a conditional proposition; it is a categorical universal with a relative clause.
The author explains that the conditional word 'لو' is inside the relative clause, not forming a separate conditional.
4
Understand Why the Conditional Reading Fails
A conditional (shartiyya) judges the existence of one proposition on the supposition of another. But here, the meaning is simply 'every thing that has the first property has the second property.' The conditional word is only used to indicate that the subject includes potential individuals, as logicians use conditionals for both actual and supposed cases.
The author gives examples like pointing to Zayd or a stone to show conditionals can refer to particular instances, not universal relations.
5
Note the Textual Correction
The author points out that some copies have 'ولو' (and if) before 'كان إنسانا', which is a scribal error. The correct reading omits the 'و' to maintain proper syntax: 'إن' would have no predicate or the conditional would lack a consequent.
This correction preserves the intended meaning and grammatical structure.

و قد علم من ذلك ان ما وقع في بعض النسخ من قولهم ان كلما لو وجد في الخارج و كان انساناً (بالواو) غلط نشأ من طغيان القلم لانه على تقدير ثبوته بقى كلمة الشرط من دون جواب او كلمة «ان» بدون خبر فان الجواب لا يعطف على الشرط وذلك ، لانه لازم و الشرط ملزوم و بينهما غاية الاتحاد وفي العطف يشترط ان لا يكون بين المتعاطفين غاية الاتحاد ولا غاية الانفصال كما صرح بذلك جماعة منه

Know that some have mistakenly imagined from the apparent words of the people that their saying 'Every time if it existed in the external world it would be a human, then it is such that if it existed it would be an animal', for example, is a conditional compound composed of two conditionals, like our saying 'Every time if the sun is rising then daytime exists, so every time daytime does not exist then the sun is not rising'. So if you delete the tools indicating connection, namely their saying 'every time' and 'then it is such that', there remains 'if it existed in the external world it would be a human and if it existed it would be an animal', and they are two conditional propositions, just as if you deleted in the mentioned example there remains 'if the sun is rising then daytime exists, and every time daytime does not exist then the sun is not rising', and they are two conditional propositions. And its corruption is not hidden, because it is apparent that this 'every time' is not a tool of connection, but rather it is two words, and 'ma' is a relative pronoun or described, and what follows it is its clause or description, and the word 'kull' is a noun, and the sentence 'then it is on the supposition of its existence in the external world an animal' is its predicate, beginning with 'fa' because the subject implies the meaning of condition. The meaning is: everything that has this first property has this second property. Moreover, we can deny that their saying 'if it existed it would be a human and if it existed it would be an animal' is a conditional, because a conditional is that in which the judgment is of establishing a relation on a supposition of another, as has preceded and will come, God willing. And we do not concede that its meaning is the establishment of humanity and animality on the supposition of something's existence in the external world, because our saying 'Every human is an animal', for example, does not indicate this. Rather, the intent is: every time the intellect assumes it is a human, it is an animal. But since the immediate understanding from our saying 'Every human is an animal' is that the judgment is about every human that actually exists in the external world, and that supposed individuals are not included under the judgment, then the comparison between the real proposition and the external proposition would not be correct. So the conditional word is brought as a warning that the intended meaning of the subject is more general than actually existing and supposed individuals. And that is because the conditional word is used by the masters of the scale (logic) for actual and supposed things, like your saying 'If this is a human, it is an animal', pointing to 'Zayd', and your saying 'If this is a human, it is an animal', pointing to 'stone'. So know this sentence and memorize it so that the situation is not confused and the discourse does not become mixed.
And it has been known from this that what occurs in some copies of their saying 'that every time if it existed in the external world and it was a human' (with waw) is a mistake arising from the slip of the pen, because on the supposition of its being established, the conditional word remains without a consequent, or the word 'an' without a predicate. For the consequent is not conjoined with the condition, because it is required and the condition is the requirer, and between them there is extreme union, and in conjunction it is necessary that between the conjoined things there is neither extreme union nor extreme disjunction, as a group of them have explicitly stated. Then he said: 'then it is on the supposition of its existence...' if it was the predicate of 'an' as intended, the conditional word remains without a consequent, and if it was the consequent of the conditional word, 'an' remains without a predicate, and both are not permissible. So it must be that his saying 'was' 'a human' without waw so that it correctly becomes a consequent of the condition, and his saying 'then it is on the supposition of its existence' becomes the predicate of 'an' as before, and no problem arises.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification about the parsing of universal propositions in logic. The author refutes a misconception by some that the phrase 'كلما لو وجد في الخارج كان إنسانا فهو بحيث لو وجد كان حيوانا' is a compound conditional (shartiyya muttasila) composed of two conditionals. He demonstrates that 'كلما' here is not a conditional tool but two words: 'كل' (every) and 'ما' (a relative pronoun or described noun). The sentence structure is: 'كل' as subject, 'ما لو وجد...' as its qualifier, and 'فهو على تقدير وجوده حيوان' as the predicate, with the 'fa' indicating a conditional sense. The author then argues that the phrases 'لو وجد كان إنسانا' and 'لو وجد كان حيوانا' are not themselves conditionals in the technical sense, because a conditional (shartiyya) judges the existence of one proposition on the supposition of another. Rather, 'كل إنسان حيوان' directly affirms animality for every human, without needing a conditional. The conditional word is introduced only to indicate that the subject includes both actual and possible individuals, as logicians use conditionals for both real and supposed cases (like pointing to Zayd or a stone). Finally, the author points out a textual error in some copies that include 'واو' (and) before 'كان إنسانا', which would break the syntax: either the conditional would lack a consequent or the 'أن' would lack a predicate. The correct reading is without the waw, so that 'كان إنسانا' is the consequent of the condition, and 'فهو على تقدير وجوده' is the predicate of 'أن'.

Condition of Possibility in Haqiqiyya Propositions
Author explains necessity of possibility condition for haqiqiyya subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the condition of possibility is necessary for haqiqiyya propositions and identify when it is not required based on alternative views.

Core Idea
The condition of possibility protects universal propositions from being falsified by impossible individuals.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical problem in the logic of universal propositions: if we include impossible individuals in the subject, the proposition becomes false. The solution is to restrict the subject to possible individuals. Yet later, al-Jurjani shows that this restriction is unnecessary if we adopt a deeper view about the truth of the descriptive attribute. This lesson will unpack the problem and the two approaches.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The author argues that if the subject of a universal proposition includes all individuals, both possible and impossible, then the proposition cannot be true. Why? Because impossible individuals can serve as counterexamples.
2
Trace the example
The example: 'Every human is an animal.' If we suppose a human that is not an animal (an impossibility), then 'Some human is not an animal' becomes true (in the sense of 'some entity that would be human if it existed is not animal'). This contradicts the universal. Similarly for 'No human is a stone'.
The same reasoning applies to any universal affirmative or negative.
3
Identify the solution
To avoid this, logicians add the condition of possibility (imkān) to the subject: the proposition is only about individuals whose existence is possible. Impossible individuals are excluded from the domain.
4
Examine al-Jurjani's refinement
Al-Jurjani notes that the condition of possibility is only needed under a particular interpretation. If we require the descriptive attribute (like 'human') to be possibly or actually true of the subject in reality (nafs al-amr), then impossible individuals are not truly instances of the attribute and are automatically excluded.
This is the view of al-Fārābī (possibility) and Ibn Sīnā (actuality).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: لانه لو اعتبر في مطلق الافراد سواء كانت ممتنعة او ممكنة لم يصدق كلية أصلاً الا موجبة ولا سالبة وذلك ، لانه اذا فرض بعض الانسان مثلاً ليس بحيوان وان كان ممتنعاً، يصدق ح: بعض الانسان ليس بحيوان بمعنى ان بعض ما لو وجد كان انساناً فهو على تقدیر وجوده ليس بحیوان و هو نقيض قولنا كل انسان حيوان فيكذب ذلك قطعاً، لان صدق احد النقيضين يستلزم كذب الاخر وكذا اذا فرض بعض افراد الانسان مثلاً حجراً و ان كان ممتعاً، يصدق بعض الانسان حجر» بالمعنى المذكور وهو نقيض قولنا: لاشيء من الانسان بحجر فيكذب ذلك البتة لما ذكر، فلذا اخذوا قيد امكان في موضوع الحقيقية، هذا.
قال المحقق الشريف: وهذا القيد اعنى امكان وجود الافراد انما يحتاج اليه اذا لم يعتبر امكان صدق الوصف العنوانى على ذات الموضوع بحسب نفس الأمر و يكتفى بمجرد فرض صدقه او امکان فرض صدقه عليه كما في صدق الكلي على جزئياته حتى اذا وقع الكلى موضوعاً للقضية الكلية كان الحكم متناولاً لجميع افراده التي هو كلى بالقياس اليها سواء امكن صدقه عليها أولا و اما اذا اعتبر امكان صدق العنوان على ذات الموضوع فى نفس الأمر كما هو مذهب الفارابى او اعتبر مع الامكان الصدق بالفعل كما هو مذهب الشيخ فلا حاجة الى اعتبار امكان وجود الافراد و المحذور مندفع فان الانسان الذي ليس بحيوان لا يصدق عليه الانسان في نفس الامر فلا يدخل فى قولنا كل انسان حیوان و كذا الانسان الذي هو حجر لا يصدق عليه الانسان في نفس الامر فلا يدخل في قولنا : «لاشيء من الانسان بحجر
They said: Because if it were considered in all individuals, whether impossible or possible, a universal proposition would not be true at all, neither affirmative nor negative. That is because if one assumes that some human, for example, is not an animal, even if it is impossible, then the proposition 'Some human is not an animal' becomes true, meaning: some of what if it existed would be a human, then on the assumption of its existence it is not an animal, and this is the contradictory of our saying 'Every human is an animal', so the latter would certainly be false, because the truth of one of two contradictories necessitates the falsity of the other. Similarly, if one assumes that some individuals of human, for example, are stone, even if it is impossible, then 'Some human is stone' becomes true in the mentioned sense, and this is the contradictory of our saying 'Nothing of human is stone', so that would be false for the reason mentioned. Therefore they added the condition of possibility (imkān) in the subject of the real (ḥaqīqiyya) proposition. This is it.
Al-Muḥaqqiq al-Sharīf said: This condition, i.e., the possibility of the existence of individuals, is only needed if one does not consider the possibility of the truth of the descriptive attribute (al-waṣf al-‘unwānī) upon the essence of the subject according to reality (bi-ḥasab nafs al-amr), and is satisfied with mere assumption of its truth or the possibility of assuming its truth upon it, as in the truth of the universal over its particulars, so that when the universal occurs as subject of a universal proposition, the judgment encompasses all its individuals, relative to which it is universal, whether it is possible for it to be true upon them or not. But if one considers the possibility of the truth of the attribute upon the essence of the subject in reality, as is the view of al-Fārābī, or considers with possibility actual truth, as is the view of al-Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā), then there is no need to consider the possibility of the existence of individuals, and the problem is resolved. For the human that is not an animal is not truly described by 'human' in reality, so it does not enter into our saying 'Every human is an animal', and similarly the human that is stone is not truly described by 'human' in reality, so it does not enter into our saying 'Nothing of human is stone'.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the condition of possibility (imkān) in the subject of ḥaqīqiyya (real) universal propositions. The author presents a standard argument: if the subject of a universal proposition were taken to include absolutely all individuals (including impossible ones like a human that is not an animal or a human that is a stone), then the proposition would be falsified. For example, consider 'Every human is an animal'. If we suppose a human that is not an animal (even if impossible), then the particular negative 'Some human is not an animal' would be true (since we can suppose such an individual), which contradicts the universal affirmative. Similarly for 'No human is a stone': if we suppose a human that is a stone, then 'Some human is a stone' would be true, contradicting the universal negative. Therefore, to avoid this, logicians restrict the subject of ḥaqīqiyya propositions to possible individuals only. The author then cites al-Muḥaqqiq al-Sharīf (al-Jurjānī), who clarifies that this condition is only needed if one does not already consider the truth of the descriptive attribute (like 'human') in reality (nafs al-amr). If one follows al-Fārābī's view that the attribute must be possibly true of the subject in reality, or Ibn Sīnā's view that it must be actually true, then impossible individuals like a human that is not an animal are not truly human at all, so they are not included in the subject, and the problem disappears. Thus, the condition of possibility is only a safeguard for those who do not adopt these stricter views on the existential import of the attribute.

Key Terms
قضية حقيقية
امكان
الوصف العنوانى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why would the universal proposition 'Every human is an animal' be false if the subject includes impossible individuals?

B) Because 'Every human is an animal' is only true for actual humans
C) Because impossible individuals are not part of the domain of logic
D) Because the universal has to be converted to a particular

Key Takeaway
The condition of possibility safeguards universal propositions from falsification by impossible individuals, but it is not needed if the descriptive attribute is already restricted to possible or actual instances in reality.

?
Explain why the condition of possibility is necessary if we adopt a simple view of the subject term, but not if we require the descriptive attribute to be true in reality.

Supposing External Existence Implies Possibility
Author corrects a mistaken statement about supposed existence applying to impossible individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between logical supposition of external existence (which implies possibility) and mental conception of impossible individuals, and explain why the former cannot apply to impossibles.

Core Idea
Supposing something to exist externally already makes it possible; impossible things cannot be supposed externally without negating their impossibility.

This passage corrects a common misunderstanding: that the 'supposed existing in the external world' applies only to possible individuals, not impossible ones like a partner of the Creator. The author argues that this correction is itself mistaken because the very act of supposing external existence presupposes possibility. Let's unpack this logical distinction and see how it affects our understanding of mental propositions in Islamic logic and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken statement
Locate in the Arabic the previous commentator's claim: that the supposed existing in the external world is only considered for possible individuals, not impossible ones like the individuals of nothingness (lā shay') or a partner of the Creator (sharīk al-bārī).
This is the claim being criticized.
2
Analyze the author's objection
The author argues that anything that can be supposed to exist externally is by definition possible. Why? Because suppressing external existence requires that actuality is not precluded, but impossible individuals are prevented from both external existence and actuality altogether.
You cannot suppose an impossible thing to exist externally without negating its impossibility.
3
Distinguish two senses of 'supposition'
The author clarifies that 'taqdīr' in logical contexts means a supposition where actuality is not far and the external world does not prevent it. However, in mental propositions (qaḍiyya dhihniyya), we can conceive impossibilities, like 'The partner of the Creator is impossible' — meaning the mental concept is qualified as impossible in the external world.
This second kind does not imply possibility of external existence.
4
Apply the distinction to the original correction
Conclude that the original correction was misplaced because it conflated these two kinds of supposition. The logical supposition of external existence always implies possibility; the mental conception does not.
This prevents invalid arguments in Kalam debates about non-existent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله «وهذا الموجود المقدر الوجود في الخارج انما اعتبروه في الافراد الممكنة لا الممتنعة كافراد اللاشيء و شريك البارى»، استدراك ليس فى محله فان كل شيء يقدر وجوده خارجاً، فهو ممكن، لان الممتنع الوجود في الخارج لا يقدر وجوده الخارجى الأمع الغاء امتناعه لامع حفظ هذا الوصف فيه، مضافاً الى ان المراد بالتقدير في مثل هذه المواضع والاستعمالات التقدير الذي لا تبعد عليه الفعلية ولا يمتنع عنه الخارج والافراد الممتنعة ممتنع عنها الخارج والفعلية جميعاً. واما على الموضوع الموجود في الذهن، كقولنا : شريك البارى ممتنع بمعنى ان كلها يوجد في العقل ويفرضه العقل شريك البارى فهو موصوف بالذهن بانه ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، وهذه تسمى الذهنية.
And his saying: 'This supposed existing being in the external world, they only considered it in possible individuals, not impossible ones, like the individuals of nothingness and the partner of the Creator' — is a correction that is not in its place. Because everything whose existence is supposed in the external world is possible, for the impossible of existence in the external world cannot be supposed to exist externally except by negating its impossibility, not by preserving this description in it. Additionally, the intended meaning of 'supposition' in such places and usages is the supposition in which actuality is not far from it and the external world does not prevent it. And impossible individuals are prevented from both external existence and actuality altogether. As for when the subject exists in the mind, like our saying: 'The partner of the Creator is impossible', meaning that everything that exists in the mind and the mind supposes it as a partner of the Creator, it is described in the mind as being impossible of existence in the external world. And these are called mental propositions (dhihniyya).
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (taṣḥīḥ) of a previous commentator's statement. The author takes issue with someone who said that the supposed existing in the external world (al-mawjūd al-muqaddar al-wujūd fī al-khārij) is only considered for possible individuals, not impossible ones like the individuals of nothingness (lā shay') or a partner of the Creator (sharīk al-bārī). The author argues that this correction is misplaced because anything that can be supposed to exist externally is by that very fact possible. To suppose an impossible thing existing externally requires negating its impossibility, not preserving it. The term 'supposition' (taqdīr) in logical contexts refers to a supposition where actuality (fi‘liyya) is not precluded and the external world does not prevent it; impossible individuals are prevented from both external existence and actuality. However, if we are talking about mental existence (as in the subject of a mental proposition), then we can say 'The partner of the Creator is impossible' — meaning that whatever the mind conceives as a partner of the Creator is mentally qualified as impossible in the external world. Such propositions are called 'mental' (dhihniyya). Thus, the correction is erroneous because it conflates two different kinds of supposition: logical supposition (which implies possibility) and mental conception (which can include impossibilities).

Key Terms
تقدير (taqdīr): Supposition of existence, which in logical contexts implies possibility of external existence.
قضية ذهنية (qaḍiyya dhihniyya): A mental proposition whose subject exists only in the mind, e.g., 'The partner of the Creator is impossible'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because impossible things cannot be supposed at all
C) Because the phrase is about mental existence, not external
D) Because the condition of possibility only applies to universal propositions

Key Takeaway
The key logical point is that the supposition of external existence (taqdīr) necessarily implies possibility, while mental propositions (qaḍiyya dhihniyya) can be about impossibles without implying possible external existence.

The Condition of Possibility in Universal Propositions
Author explains the need for the condition of possibility of individuals in subject terms and compares views of Farabi and Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the condition of possibility of individuals is needed in a universal proposition and distinguish the positions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
The need for the condition depends on whether we consider real application of the subject term.

In classical logic, universal propositions like 'Every human is an animal' are meant to cover all possible individuals of the subject. But what about impossible individuals, like a human that is not an animal? The standard solution is to add a condition that the subject's individuals must be possible. However, this condition is not always necessary. This passage, quoting al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, explains that if we adopt the views of al-Farabi or Ibn Sina, the condition becomes redundant because their approaches naturally exclude impossible cases. Understanding this subtle point clarifies how different logical frameworks handle the scope of universal judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition under discussion
The condition is 'imkan wujud al-afrad' (possibility of existence of individuals). It is a safeguard to ensure that a universal proposition only applies to possible individuals of the subject, not to impossible ones (e.g., 'human that is not an animal').
This condition appears in the standard treatment of universal propositions in logic.
2
Understand when the condition is needed
The passage states that the condition is needed only if we do not consider the real applicability of the descriptive term to the subject, but instead are satisfied with merely assuming its application. Under this assumption, the proposition would range over all individuals, whether possible or not, so the condition is required to exclude impossible ones.
3
Contrast al-Farabi's approach
Al-Farabi's doctrine considers the possibility of the descriptive term applying to the subject in reality (bi hasab nafs al-amr). Under this view, impossible individuals like 'human that is not an animal' do not even qualify as instances of 'human', so they are automatically excluded. No separate condition is needed.
4
Contrast Ibn Sina's approach
Ibn Sina's doctrine goes further: it considers actual application (al-sidq bi al-fi'l) along with possibility. Again, impossible individuals are excluded because the term 'human' does not actually apply to them in reality.
Both al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, by grounding the subject term in reality, eliminate the need for the explicit condition of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المحقق الشريف وهذا القيد اعنى امكان وجود الافراد انما يحتاج اليه اذا لم يعتبر امكان صدق الوصف العنوانى على ذات الموضوع بحسب نفس الأمر بل يكتفى بمجرد فرض صدقه او امکان فرض صدقه عليه كما في صدق الكلي على جزئياته حتى اذا وقع الكلى موضوعاً للقضية الكلية كان الحكم متناولاً تجميع افراده التي هو كلى بالقياس اليها سواء امكن صدقه عليها أولا و اما اذا اعتبر امكان صدق العنوان على ذات الموضوع فى نفس الأمر كما هو مذهب الفارابى او اعتبر مع الامكان الصدق بالفعل كما هو مذهب الشيخ فلا حاجة الى اعتبار امكان وجود الافراد و المحذور مندفع فان الانسان الذي ليس بحيوان لا يصدق عليه الانسان في نفس الامر فلا يدخل فى قولنا كل انسان حیوان و كذا الانسان الذي هو حجر لا يصدق عليه الانسان في نفس الامر فلا يدخل في قولنا : «لاشيء من الانسان بحجر
Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: This condition, namely the possibility of existence of individuals, is only needed if one does not consider the possibility of the descriptive term applying to the subject in reality, but rather is satisfied with merely assuming its application or the possibility of assuming its application to it, as in the application of the universal to its particulars, so that when the universal occurs as the subject of a universal proposition, the judgment encompasses all its individuals, to which it is universal in relation, whether its application to them is possible or not. But if one considers the possibility of the term applying to the subject in reality, as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, or considers actual application along with possibility, as is the doctrine of the Master (Ibn Sina), then there is no need to consider the possibility of individuals, and the difficulty is avoided. For the human that is not an animal does not have 'human' apply to it in reality, so it does not enter into our statement 'Every human is an animal'; likewise, the human that is a stone does not have 'human' apply to it in reality, so it does not enter into our statement 'Nothing of the human is a stone'.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an earlier point about the condition (qayd) of possibility of individuals in universal propositions. The author, quoting al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, explains that this condition is only necessary if we do not consider the reality of the descriptive term's application to the subject. The standard view in logic is that a universal proposition like 'Every human is an animal' applies to all possible humans, not just existing ones. However, if we adopt the doctrine of al-Farabi, who considers the possibility of the term applying in reality, or the doctrine of Ibn Sina, who considers actual application (along with possibility), then impossible individuals like 'human that is not an animal' are excluded naturally because they do not satisfy the term 'human' in reality. Thus, no separate condition is needed.

Key Terms
إمكان وجود الأفراد
الوصف العنواني
الفارابي
الشيخ (ابن سينا)

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The doctrine that requires actual existence of all individuals.

Key Takeaway
The condition of possibility of individuals in universal propositions is only needed when we do not ground the subject term in reality; al-Farabi and Ibn Sina both avoid the need by requiring real applicability of the term.

Distinguishing Haqiqiyya, Kharijiyya, and Dhihniyya Propositions
Author critiques a mistaken correction and establishes the threefold division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition as haqiqiyya, kharijiyya, or dhihniyya based on the existence status of its subject.

Core Idea
Propositions are divided by how their subject relates to existence: actual, potential, or mental only.

The classical division of propositions into haqiqiyya (real) and kharijiyya (external) was found insufficient for propositions about impossible subjects like 'the partner of God'. The author introduces the third category: dhihniyya (mental). Understanding these three types is crucial for correctly interpreting qaḍāyā in logic and theology. This passage refines the classification and resolves a potential confusion about mental propositions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two classical divisions
Recall that a haqiqiyya proposition considers the predicate for everything the subject applies to, whether existing or potential. A kharijiyya proposition restricts the subject to actually existing external individuals.
Example: 'Every human is animal' is haqiqiyya; 'Every human in Mecca is standing' is kharijiyya.
2
Identify the problem with impossible subjects
Propositions like 'The partner of God is impossible' have subjects with no possible external existence. They cannot be haqiqiyya (which requires possibility) nor kharijiyya (which requires actuality).
The scholars originally only recognized these two, so an objection arose.
3
Learn the third category: dhihniyya
For such cases, the author defines a mental proposition: the subject exists only in the mind, and the predicate predicates something about external reality (e.g., impossibility) on that mental concept.
Example: 'The partner of God is impossible' means 'Everything that exists in the mind and is assumed as partner of God is described in the mind as being impossible in external reality.'
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction
When we say 'The partner of God is impossible in the mind', the impossibility refers to external impossibility, not mental. Thus, the proposition is meaningful and not contradictory.
The addition of 'in external reality' in some copies makes this explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(التقريب ص ٤٤) (۵۵) تمثيل للممتنعة لا الممكنة كما هو ظاهر. (محمد على) (۵۶) اعلم ان القوم قسموا القضية الى الخارجية و الحقيقية ولم يلتفتوا إلى الذهنية فأورد عليهم بان هيهنا قضايا وهى ليست الموضوعاتها افراد ممكنة التحقق خارجة عن الخارجية اذ ليس افراد موضوعها محققاً الحقيقية، اذ لا يمكن وجود افراد موضوعها في الخارج وقد اعتبر في الحقيقية امكان في الخارج و عن وجود الأفراد كمامر، فذهب المصنف الى ان هذه القضايا ذهنية، فجعل القضية ثلاثة اقسام حقيقية وخارجية وذهنية (عبدالرحيم) (۵۷) قوله فهو موصوف في الذهن بالامتناع في الخارج» ربما يتوهم: ان صدر هذا الكلام ينا في عجزه فان صدره صريح في ان شريك البارى موجود في الذهن و عجزه يدل على امتناع ذلك و ماهو الاتهافت اذلا معنى لقولنا الذى في الذهن ممتنع في الذهن). في والجواب انه ليس المراد بالامتناع ، الامتناع الذهنى بل الخارجى ومعنى الكلام ان كلما يوجد في الذهن و يصدق عليه شريك البارى فهو موصوف في الذهن بكونه ممتنعاً في الخارج و ان كان موجوداً الذهن وكذا قولنا : كل ممتنع معدوم معناه ان كلما يوجد في الذهن ويصدق عليه الممتنع فهو معدوم في الخارج و هكذا كلما يوهم بظاهره ذلك من امثلة القضية الذهنية ويدل على ما ذكر ما وقع في بعض النسخ من زيادة قولنا : «في الخارج» بعد قولنا : «بالامتناع فانه صريح فيما ذكرنا . (ميرزا محمد على)
Shaykh Abd al-Rahim said, after investigating this topic in summary: Then this section is only called 'real' (haqiqi) because every proposition has a reality, which is the truth of the predicate of that to which the subject applies, whether actually or potentially. So if nothing beyond this consideration is taken into account, it is in its reality. (54) And his statement: 'This estimated existent in external reality is only considered by them in possible individuals, not impossible ones like the individuals of 'nothing' and the partner of God' is an incorrect correction. For everything whose existence in external reality is estimated is possible, because that whose existence in external reality is impossible cannot have its external existence estimated except by canceling its impossibility, not while preserving this description in it. Moreover, what is meant by 'estimation' in such places and usages is the estimation that does not preclude actuality and does not prevent external reality. Impossible individuals are prevented from both external reality and actuality. As for the subject that exists in the mind, like our statement: 'The partner of God is impossible', meaning that everything that exists in the mind and the mind assumes it as partner of God, it is described in the mind as being impossible of existence in external reality. These are called 'mental' (dhihniyya). And his statement: 'And this they only considered in subjects that have no individuals possibly actualized in external reality' is also an incorrect correction like its predecessor. For what exists in the mind and is assumed to be impossible cannot be conceived to have individuals possibly actualized in external reality. For the mind's assumption is not a wish, since wishes have no return, and one may not formulate from them scientific principles that are considered a general criterion applicable in the investigations of the sciences. (al-Taqrib p.44) (55) An example of the impossible, not the possible, as is apparent. (Muhammad Ali) (56) Know that the scholars divided the proposition into external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) and did not pay attention to the mental (dhihniyya). So it was objected to them that here are propositions whose subjects do not have individuals possibly actualized, beyond the external (since the individuals of their subject are not actualized) and not real, since the existence of individuals of their subject cannot be in external reality, and possibility in external reality and existence of individuals has been considered in the real, as before. So the author (al-Muhyi?) went to the view that these propositions are mental, thus making the proposition three divisions: real, external, and mental. (Abd al-Rahim) (57) His statement: 'So it is described in the mind as being impossible in external reality' might give the illusion that the beginning of this statement contradicts its end, for its beginning is explicit that the partner of God exists in the mind, and its end indicates the impossibility of that. What a contradiction! Because there is no meaning to our statement: 'That which is in the mind is impossible in the mind.' The answer is that what is meant by impossibility is not mental impossibility but external impossibility. The meaning of the statement is that everything that exists in the mind and is predicated as 'partner of God' is described in the mind as being impossible in external reality, even though it exists in the mind. Similarly, our statement: 'Every impossible thing is non-existent' means that everything that exists in the mind and is predicated as 'impossible' is non-existent in external reality. And so on for every example that gives that illusion in its apparent meaning from the examples of mental propositions. What indicates what we have mentioned is what occurs in some copies of an addition of our statement 'in external reality' after our statement 'the impossibility', for it is explicit in what we have mentioned. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement and definitional classification. It begins with Shaykh Abd al-Rahim explaining why the term 'haqiqi' (real) is used for the division of propositions that consider the reality of the predicate applying to whatever the subject applies to, without additional conditions. Then the author refutes an incorrect correction (istidrak) that claimed estimated existence is only for possible individuals. The author argues that estimated existence always implies possibility—impossible things cannot be estimated without losing their impossibility. The real purpose of estimation is to consider possible existence. The passage then introduces the threefold division: external (kharijiyya), real (haqiqiyya), and mental (dhihniyya). The scholars originally only recognized external and real, but it was objected that some propositions (e.g., 'The partner of God is impossible') have subjects that cannot have possible external individuals. The author therefore added the mental division. Finally, a potential contradiction is resolved: when we say 'the partner of God is impossible in the mind', the impossibility refers to external impossibility, not mental impossibility. Thus, the mental proposition predicates external impossibility of a mental subject.

ذهنية

Key Takeaway
Propositions with impossible subjects are classified as mental (dhihniyya), where the subject exists in the mind and the predicate describes external impossibility.

Classification of Propositions into Simple and Modified
Author details the division of propositions into muhassala and ma'dula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as muhassala or ma'dula based on the position of the negation particle, and identify the specific subtype among the six ma'dula varieties.

Core Idea
The negation particle's position determines whether a proposition is simple or modified.

In this passage, the author provides a detailed parsing of propositions based on the presence and placement of the negation particle (harf al-salb). This classification is foundational for logical analysis in Islamic sciences, as it allows you to correctly identify the logical form of statements. By learning to distinguish muhassala from ma'dula propositions, you gain precision in handling arguments in fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify presence of negation particle
Look at the proposition. Does it contain a word like 'not' (لا, ليس, etc.)? If no, it is muhassala affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is standing').
2
Determine if negation is inside or outside
If the proposition includes negation, check whether the negation is part of the subject or predicate term (internal) or a separate operator (external). If external, it is saliba muhassala (negative simple, e.g., 'Zayd is not standing').
In the negative simple, the negation is not a part of the subject or predicate term but operates on the whole proposition.
3
Locate the negation's position for internal cases
If negation is internal, it is ma'dula. Determine whether it modifies the subject (e.g., 'Non-Zayd is standing'), the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is non-standing'), or both (e.g., 'Non-Zayd is non-standing').
4
Determine the judgment type
For each ma'dula proposition, check the overall judgment: affirmative (positive copula) or negative (negative copula). This gives six subtypes (3 positions × 2 judgments).
For example, 'Non-Zayd is standing' is affirmative; 'Non-Zayd is not standing' is negative.
5
Distinguish from negative simple
Compare an affirmative ma'dula (e.g., 'Non-Zayd is standing') with a negative simple ('Zayd is not standing'). Both have one negation, but in the ma'dula it is part of the subject term, while in the negative simple it is outside. This difference is crucial.
The author emphasizes this distinction to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «فالقضية على الأول تسمى معدولة الموضوع ...» والتفصيل في هذا المقام: ان القضية اما ان تكون مشتملة على حرف السلب ام لا، الثانية هي الموجبة المحصلة كقولنا: «زيد قائم» و الاولى اما ان يجعل حرف السلب جزء من جزء منها ام لا، الثانية هي السالبة المحصلة و الاولى اما ان يكون حرف السلب جزء من احد طرفيها او من كليهما، والاولى اما ان يكون جزء من موضوعها او من محمولها وهذه الثلاثة الاخيرة اما ان يكون الحكم فيها بالايجاب أو بالسلب فهذه سنة اقسام ثلاثة منها موجبة معدولة وثلاثة منها سالبة معدولة، وقول المصنف : و قد يجعل حرف السلب جزء من جزء منها فتسمى معدولة» يشتمل على جميع هذه الاقسام الستة و ان كان المتبادر منه هو الثلاثة الأول وكذا قوله : والا فمحصلة على ما فى اكثر النسخ، يشتمل القسمين الأولين و ان كان المتبادر هو الثاني. والفرق بين هذه القضايا الثمانية المذكورة ظاهر معنى و كذا لفظاً الأبين الموجبة المعدولة الموضوع او المحمول و بين السالبة المحصلة اى) التي حرف السلب في صدرها أو اثنائها والاول على الأول و الثاني على الثانى والا فلا اشتباه فى العكس فلاحظ فان كل واحدة منهما مشتملة على حرف سلب واحد والابين الموجبة المعدولة الطرفين و السائبة المعدولة الموضوع او المحمول (اى): السائبة المعدولة الموضوع التي تكون حرف السلب فى اثنائها والا فلا اشتباه، ضرورة ان الموضوع هنا حينئذ تكون فيه حرفا سلب والمحمول لا تكون فيه حرف سلب اصلاً بخلاف الموجبة المعدولة الطرفين فان في كل واحد من موضوعها
His statement: 'So the proposition, according to the first, is called 'modified in subject'...' The detail in this topic is that the proposition either includes the negation particle or not. The second is the affirmative simple (muhassala) like our statement: 'Zayd is standing'. The first (i.e., including negation) either makes the negation particle a part of a part of it or not. The second (i.e., making negation not a part of a part) is the negative simple. The first (i.e., making negation a part of a part) either makes the negation particle a part of one of its two sides (subject or predicate) or of both. The first of these (negation in one side) either makes it a part of its subject or of its predicate. These last three (negation in subject, in predicate, or in both) have the judgment either as affirmative or negative. So these are six divisions: three affirmative modified and three negative modified. The author's statement: 'And sometimes the negation particle is made a part of a part of it, and then it is called modified' encompasses all these six divisions, although what is immediately understood is the first three. Similarly, his statement: 'Otherwise it is simple' in most copies encompasses the first two divisions, although what is immediately understood is the second. The difference between these eight mentioned propositions is apparent in meaning and likewise in wording, except between the affirmative modified in subject or predicate and the negative simple (i.e., that in which the negation particle is at its beginning or in its middle) — the first alternative is for the first, the second for the second; otherwise there is no confusion in the opposite. Consider, for each of them includes one negation particle. And the difference between the affirmative modified in both sides and the negative modified in subject or predicate (i.e., the negative modified in subject that has the negation particle in its middle) is clear; otherwise there is no confusion. Necessarily, the subject here then has a negation particle, and the predicate has no negation particle at all, unlike the affirmative modified in both sides, for in each of its subject...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of propositions based on the inclusion and placement of the negation particle (harf al-salb). The author explains that a proposition either does not contain negation (affirmative simple, like 'Zayd is standing') or contains negation. If it contains negation, it either makes the negation a part of a part (like modifying the subject or predicate) or not (negative simple, like 'Zayd is not standing'). The latter is the negative simple (saliba muhassala). When negation is made a part of a part, it becomes modified (ma'dula). There are three positions for the negation: in the subject, in the predicate, or in both. Each can be affirmative or negative in judgment, yielding six modified propositions: e.g., affirmative modified in subject ('Non-Zayd is standing'), affirmative modified in predicate ('Zayd is non-standing'), affirmative modified in both ('Non-Zayd is non-standing'), and their negative counterparts. The author clarifies potential confusion between the affirmative modified in subject/predicate and the negative simple, both of which have only one negation particle. The difference is that in the negative simple, the negation is outside the subject-predicate structure (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing'), while in the modified, the negation is inside the term itself (e.g., 'non-Zayd'). The text distinguishes eight types: two simple (affirmative and negative) and six modified (three affirmative, three negative).

Key Takeaway
The eight types of propositions reduce to two simple (affirmative and negative) and six modified (three positions × two judgments), distinguished by the placement of the negation particle relative to the terms.

Defining Modified Negative and Simple Negative Propositions
Defines and contrasts two types of negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a modified negative proposition (saliba al-mahmul) and a simple negative proposition (saliba muhassala) in Arabic.

Core Idea
Form is not enough: modified negatives are affirmative in logical structure despite having a negative form.

When we say 'Zaid is not-a-writer' (saliba al-mahmul), we affirm a negative property of Zaid; when we say 'Zaid is not a writer' (saliba muhassala), we simply negate the predicate. This crucial distinction affects whether the proposition requires the subject to exist. Let's unpack this foundational idea in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look at the Arabic construction: If the negative particle (لا) is attached to the predicate making it one term (e.g., لاكاتب), it is modified negative. If the negation is separate (e.g., ليس بكاتب), it is simple negative.
Modified negatives are affirmative in form but have a negative predicate.
2
Determine the logical meaning
For modified negative: the predicate is a negative term established for the subject. For simple negative: the predicate is negated from the subject entirely.
3
Check existential import
Modified negatives require subject existence because they affirm a property. Simple negatives do not require existence because they merely negate.
This is the author's initial claim before the later discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التصديقات
اما الاول فلان معنى قولنا زيد لاكاتب هو ان اللاكاتب ثابت لزيد و كلما صدق اللاكاتب ان يقال: زيد ليس بكاتب و الا لصدق الكاتب على زيد هذا خلف.
و اما الثاني فلان معنى قولنا زيد ليس بكاتب هو ان الكتابة مسلوبة عن زيد وهذا يصدق على زيد الموجود والمعدوم بخلاف قولنا زيد لاكاتب فانه لا يصدق الا على الموجود، ضرورة ان اثبات شيء لغيره فرع وجود المثبت له سواء كان ذلك الشيء امراً وجودياً أو عدمياً.
فان قلت لم لم يلتفتوا إلى بيان النسبة بين معدولة المحمول و الموجبة المحصلة و بين السالبة المحصلة والموجبة المحصلة؟
قلت: لانه التباس بين قضيتين منها .
ثم اعلم : ان المتأخرين اثبتوا قضية سالبة المحمول وفرقوا بين موجبتها وبين السالبة المحصلة بان السالبة المحصلة يتصور فيها الطرفان والنسبة فيحكم بالسلب و في سالبة المحمول بعد تصور ما ذكر والحكم بالسلب يرجع فيحمل ذلك السلب على الموضوع، قالوا: ومعنى السالبة المحمول هو: ان الانسان شيء سلب عنه الناطق و معنى سالبة الطرفين هو ان شيئاً سلب عنه الانسان هو شيء سلب عنه الناطق، و معنى السالبة المحصلة هو: ان الانسان سلب عنه الناطق والموجبة سالبة المحمول لا يقتضى وجود الموضوع لان صدق ثبوت السلب مثل صدق السلب فكما ان الثاني لا يقتضيه ، فكذا الاول.
قال المحقق الشريف: صدق الموجبة سالبة سالبة المحمول لئلا يقتضى وجود الموضوع لان حقيقتها راجعة إلى معنى السالبة ضرورة ان انتفاء شيء عن آخر يستلزم اتصاف الاخر بانتفاء ذلك الشيء عنه و بالعكس بل لا اختلاف بينها الا بالاعتبار ولاشك ان صدق السالبة لا يقتضي وجود الموضوع فهكذا ما يلازمها يعني كما ان انتفاء المحمول عن الموضوع لا يقتضى وجود الموضوع حال الانتفاء، كذلك اتصاف الموضوع بانتفاء المحمول لا يقتضى وجوده حال الاتصاف بهذا الانتفاء لانه لازم مساوقه. و فيه ان قولهم ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له قضية بديهية أولية يحكم بها بديهية العقل ولا يستثنى العقل منها الأمر السلبي، والقول بان العقل يستثنى سالبة المحمول دون معدولة المحمول تحكم. فالحق ان الموجبة مطلقا يقتضى وجود الموضوع ولا فرق فى ذلك بين معدولة المحمول و سالبة المحمول لان المقتضى لوجود الموضوع هو دخول رابطة الايجاب والاثبات و ان كان المحمول سلبياً على انه يلزم مما ذكروه ان لا يقتضى الموجبة معدولة المحمول ايضاً وجود الموضوع، فقولهم : الموجبة تقتضى وجود الموضوع باق على اطلاقه ولم يخصص بالموجبة سالبة المحمول وكذا قولهم السالبة لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع، باق على اطلاقه ولم يخصص بالسالبة سالبة المحمول (عبد الرحيم)
On assents. As for the first, because the meaning of our saying 'Zaid is not-a-writer' is that not-writer is established for Zaid, and whenever not-writer is true, it is said: 'Zaid is not a writer', otherwise 'writer' would be true of Zaid, which is absurd. As for the second, because the meaning of our saying 'Zaid is not a writer' is that writing is negated from Zaid, and this is true of Zaid existing and non-existing, unlike our saying 'Zaid is not-a-writer', for it is only true of the existent, necessarily because establishing something for something else is a branch of the existence of that for which it is established, whether that thing is existential or non-existent. If you say: Why did they not attend to explaining the relationship between the modified predicate and the simple positive, and between the simple negative and the simple positive? I say: Because of confusion between two of those propositions. Then know: the later scholars affirmed the proposition with negated predicate and distinguished between its positive and the simple negative by stating that in the simple negative the two terms and the relation are conceived, then the negation is judged; while in the proposition with negated predicate, after conceiving what was mentioned and judging the negation, it is returned and that negation is predicated of the subject. They said: The meaning of the proposition with negated predicate is that 'man is something from which 'rational' is negated'; the meaning of the proposition with both terms negated is that 'something from which man is negated is something from which 'rational' is negated'; and the meaning of the simple negative is that 'man has 'rational' negated from him'. The positive proposition with negated predicate does not require the existence of the subject, because the truth of establishing the negation is like the truth of the negation, so just as the second does not require it, so too the first. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: The truth of the positive proposition with negated predicate does not require the existence of the subject, because its essence reduces to the meaning of the negative, necessarily because the non-existence of something from another entails the other's being characterized by the non-existence of that thing from it and vice versa; rather there is no difference between them except by consideration, and there is no doubt that the truth of the negative does not require the existence of the subject, and so that which is inseparable from it [does not require it either]. That is, just as the non-existence of the predicate from the subject does not require the existence of the subject at the time of non-existence, so too the subject's being characterized by the non-existence of the predicate does not require its existence at the time of being characterized by this non-existence, because it is a necessary consequent of that. But there is the objection that their saying 'the establishment of something for something is a branch of the existence of that for which it is established' is an a priori self-evident proposition judged by the self-evidence of the intellect, and the intellect does not exempt from it the negative case; and the claim that the intellect exempts the proposition with negated predicate without the modified predicate is arbitrary. So the truth is that the positive proposition absolutely requires the existence of the subject, and there is no difference in that between the modified predicate and the negated predicate, because what requires the existence of the subject is the entry of the copula of affirmation and establishment, even if the predicate is negative. And it follows from what they said that the positive with modified predicate also does not require the existence of the subject. So their saying 'the positive requires the existence of the subject' remains general and is not specified to the positive with negated predicate, and likewise their saying 'the negative does not require the existence of the subject' remains general and is not specified to the negative with negated predicate. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines and contrasts two types of negative propositions: the 'modified negative' (saliba al-mahmul) like 'Zaid is not-a-writer' (Zayd la-katib) and the 'simple negative' (saliba muhassala) like 'Zaid is not a writer' (Zayd laysa bi-katib). The author begins by explaining the meaning of each. For the first, 'Zaid is not-a-writer' means that the property of 'not-writer' is affirmed of Zaid. This entails that Zaid exists, because affirmation of a property requires the subject to exist. In contrast, 'Zaid is not a writer' means that writing is negated from Zaid, and this can be true whether Zaid exists or does not exist; it is a simple negation without existential import. The author then addresses a possible question: why did earlier logicians not discuss the relationship between these proposition types? The answer is that there is confusion between two of them. Next, the author explains how later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun) established the 'saliba al-mahmul' (proposition with negated predicate) and distinguished it from the simple negative. In a simple negative, the subject, predicate, and relation are all conceived, and then a negation is applied. In the negated-predicate proposition, after conceiving the subject and predicate, the negation is made part of the predicate and is then affirmed of the subject. The author gives three examples: (1) 'man is something from which 'rational' is negated' (saliba al-mahmul), (2) 'something from which man is negated is something from which 'rational' is negated' (salibat al-tarafayn, with both terms negated), and (3) 'man has 'rational' negated from him' (simple negative). The key point is that the positive form of saliba al-mahmul does not require the subject's existence, because affirming the negation is equivalent to the negation itself, and the negation does not require existence. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif supports this view by arguing that the essence of the positive with negated predicate reduces to a negative, and a negative does not presuppose existence. However, the author critiques this position with an objection: the principle that 'affirmation of something for something is a branch of the subject's existence' is an a priori truth that the intellect does not exempt for negative cases. Therefore, the author concludes that any positive proposition, whether the predicate is modified or negated, does require the subject's existence, because the affirmative copula itself demands existence. The simple negative, however, does not require existence. Hence, the general rules—'positive requires existence' and 'negative does not require existence'—remain universal and are not restricted to specific types of propositions.

Key Terms

سالبة محصلة
وجود الموضوع

Key Takeaway
Modified negatives affirm a negative predicate and thus require subject existence; simple negatives do not.

Later Logicians' Distinction and al-Sharif's Argument
Presents later logicians' and al-Sharif's argument about existential import.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif's argument that the positive form of saliba al-mahmul does not require subject existence.

Core Idea
Al-Sharif reduces the positive form to a negative, thus denying its existential import.

Earlier we learned that modified negatives require existence. But later logicians and al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif argued otherwise: they claimed that the affirmative form of saliba al-mahmul is essentially a negative and therefore does not presuppose the subject's existence. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the later logicians' view
They made a distinction between simple negative (conceive terms and then negate) and modified negative (conceive terms, negate, then affirm the negation of the subject).
Examples: 'Man is something from which rational is negated' (saliba al-mahmul) vs 'Man has rational negated from him' (simple negative).
2
Trace al-Sharif's reduction argument
Al-Sharif argues that the positive saliba al-mahmul ('Zaid is not-a-writer') is equivalent to the negative 'Zaid is not a writer' because establishing the negation (thubut al-salb) is the same as the negation itself (al-salb). Thus, its truth conditions mirror those of the simple negative.
He says 'there is no difference except by consideration (i'tibar)'.
3
Identify the key premise
Al-Sharif's premise: 'The truth of establishing the negation is like the truth of the negation.' Therefore, if the simple negative does not require subject existence, neither does the modified positive form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

و اما الثاني فلان معنى قولنا زيد ليس بكاتب هو ان الكتابة مسلوبة عن زيد وهذا يصدق على زيد الموجود والمعدوم بخلاف قولنا زيد لاكاتب فانه لا يصدق الا على الموجود، ضرورة ان اثبات شيء لغيره فرع وجود المثبت له سواء كان ذلك الشيء امراً وجودياً أو عدمياً.
فان قلت لم لم يلتفتوا إلى بيان النسبة بين معدولة المحمول و الموجبة المحصلة و بين السالبة المحصلة والموجبة المحصلة؟
قلت: لانه التباس بين قضيتين منها .
ثم اعلم : ان المتأخرين اثبتوا قضية سالبة المحمول وفرقوا بين موجبتها وبين السالبة المحصلة بان السالبة المحصلة يتصور فيها الطرفان والنسبة فيحكم بالسلب و في سالبة المحمول بعد تصور ما ذكر والحكم بالسلب يرجع فيحمل ذلك السلب على الموضوع، قالوا: ومعنى السالبة المحمول هو: ان الانسان شيء سلب عنه الناطق و معنى سالبة الطرفين هو ان شيئاً سلب عنه الانسان هو شيء سلب عنه الناطق، و معنى السالبة المحصلة هو: ان الانسان سلب عنه الناطق والموجبة سالبة المحمول لا يقتضى وجود الموضوع لان صدق ثبوت السلب مثل صدق السلب فكما ان الثاني لا يقتضيه ، فكذا الاول.
قال المحقق الشريف: صدق الموجبة سالبة سالبة المحمول لئلا يقتضى وجود الموضوع لان حقيقتها راجعة إلى معنى السالبة ضرورة ان انتفاء شيء عن آخر يستلزم اتصاف الاخر بانتفاء ذلك الشيء عنه و بالعكس بل لا اختلاف بينها الا بالاعتبار ولاشك ان صدق السالبة لا يقتضي وجود الموضوع فهكذا ما يلازمها يعني كما ان انتفاء المحمول عن الموضوع لا يقتضى وجود الموضوع حال الانتفاء، كذلك اتصاف الموضوع بانتفاء المحمول لا يقتضى وجوده حال الاتصاف بهذا الانتفاء لانه لازم مساوقه. و فيه ان قولهم ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له قضية بديهية أولية يحكم بها بديهية العقل ولا يستثنى العقل منها الأمر السلبي، والقول بان العقل يستثنى سالبة المحمول دون معدولة المحمول تحكم. فالحق ان الموجبة مطلقا يقتضى وجود الموضوع ولا فرق فى ذلك بين معدولة المحمول و سالبة المحمول لان المقتضى لوجود الموضوع هو دخول رابطة الايجاب والاثبات و ان كان المحمول سلبياً على انه يلزم مما ذكروه ان لا يقتضى الموجبة معدولة المحمول ايضاً وجود الموضوع، فقولهم : الموجبة تقتضى وجود الموضوع باق على اطلاقه ولم يخصص بالموجبة سالبة المحمول وكذا قولهم السالبة لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع، باق على اطلاقه ولم يخصص بالسالبة سالبة المحمول (عبد الرحيم)
On assents. As for the first, because the meaning of our saying 'Zaid is not-a-writer' is that not-writer is established for Zaid, and whenever not-writer is true, it is said: 'Zaid is not a writer', otherwise 'writer' would be true of Zaid, which is absurd. As for the second, because the meaning of our saying 'Zaid is not a writer' is that writing is negated from Zaid, and this is true of Zaid existing and non-existing, unlike our saying 'Zaid is not-a-writer', for it is only true of the existent, necessarily because establishing something for something else is a branch of the existence of that for which it is established, whether that thing is existential or non-existent. If you say: Why did they not attend to explaining the relationship between the modified predicate and the simple positive, and between the simple negative and the simple positive? I say: Because of confusion between two of those propositions. Then know: the later scholars affirmed the proposition with negated predicate and distinguished between its positive and the simple negative by stating that in the simple negative the two terms and the relation are conceived, then the negation is judged; while in the proposition with negated predicate, after conceiving what was mentioned and judging the negation, it is returned and that negation is predicated of the subject. They said: The meaning of the proposition with negated predicate is that 'man is something from which 'rational' is negated'; the meaning of the proposition with both terms negated is that 'something from which man is negated is something from which 'rational' is negated'; and the meaning of the simple negative is that 'man has 'rational' negated from him'. The positive proposition with negated predicate does not require the existence of the subject, because the truth of establishing the negation is like the truth of the negation, so just as the second does not require it, so too the first. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: The truth of the positive proposition with negated predicate does not require the existence of the subject, because its essence reduces to the meaning of the negative, necessarily because the non-existence of something from another entails the other's being characterized by the non-existence of that thing from it and vice versa; rather there is no difference between them except by consideration, and there is no doubt that the truth of the negative does not require the existence of the subject, and so that which is inseparable from it [does not require it either]. That is, just as the non-existence of the predicate from the subject does not require the existence of the subject at the time of non-existence, so too the subject's being characterized by the non-existence of the predicate does not require its existence at the time of being characterized by this non-existence, because it is a necessary consequent of that. But there is the objection that their saying 'the establishment of something for something is a branch of the existence of that for which it is established' is an a priori self-evident proposition judged by the self-evidence of the intellect, and the intellect does not exempt from it the negative case; and the claim that the intellect exempts the proposition with negated predicate without the modified predicate is arbitrary. So the truth is that the positive proposition absolutely requires the existence of the subject, and there is no difference in that between the modified predicate and the negated predicate, because what requires the existence of the subject is the entry of the copula of affirmation and establishment, even if the predicate is negative. And it follows from what they said that the positive with modified predicate also does not require the existence of the subject. So their saying 'the positive requires the existence of the subject' remains general and is not specified to the positive with negated predicate, and likewise their saying 'the negative does not require the existence of the subject' remains general and is not specified to the negative with negated predicate. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines and contrasts two types of negative propositions: the 'modified negative' (saliba al-mahmul) like 'Zaid is not-a-writer' (Zayd la-katib) and the 'simple negative' (saliba muhassala) like 'Zaid is not a writer' (Zayd laysa bi-katib). The author begins by explaining the meaning of each. For the first, 'Zaid is not-a-writer' means that the property of 'not-writer' is affirmed of Zaid. This entails that Zaid exists, because affirmation of a property requires the subject to exist. In contrast, 'Zaid is not a writer' means that writing is negated from Zaid, and this can be true whether Zaid exists or does not exist; it is a simple negation without existential import. The author then addresses a possible question: why did earlier logicians not discuss the relationship between these proposition types? The answer is that there is confusion between two of them. Next, the author explains how later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun) established the 'saliba al-mahmul' (proposition with negated predicate) and distinguished it from the simple negative. In a simple negative, the subject, predicate, and relation are all conceived, and then a negation is applied. In the negated-predicate proposition, after conceiving the subject and predicate, the negation is made part of the predicate and is then affirmed of the subject. The author gives three examples: (1) 'man is something from which 'rational' is negated' (saliba al-mahmul), (2) 'something from which man is negated is something from which 'rational' is negated' (salibat al-tarafayn, with both terms negated), and (3) 'man has 'rational' negated from him' (simple negative). The key point is that the positive form of saliba al-mahmul does not require the subject's existence, because affirming the negation is equivalent to the negation itself, and the negation does not require existence. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif supports this view by arguing that the essence of the positive with negated predicate reduces to a negative, and a negative does not presuppose existence. However, the author critiques this position with an objection: the principle that 'affirmation of something for something is a branch of the subject's existence' is an a priori truth that the intellect does not exempt for negative cases. Therefore, the author concludes that any positive proposition, whether the predicate is modified or negated, does require the subject's existence, because the affirmative copula itself demands existence. The simple negative, however, does not require existence. Hence, the general rules—'positive requires existence' and 'negative does not require existence'—remain universal and are not restricted to specific types of propositions.

Key Terms

ثبوت السلب
المحقق الشريف

Critique of al-Sharif: Reaffirming Universal Existential Import
Author critiques al-Sharif and defends universal existential import for positive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the author's critique of al-Sharif and articulate the author's own position that all positive propositions require subject existence.

Core Idea
The copula of affirmation itself, not the predicate's content, drives existential import.

Al-Sharif argued that the positive form of saliba al-mahmul does not require existence. The author of our passage disagrees, invoking a self-evident principle: affirming anything of something depends on that something's existence. This principle, he argues, admits no exception for negative predicates. Let's see his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's objection
The author cites the principle 'establishing something for something is a branch of the existence of that for which it is established' as a self-evident truth. He argues that the intellect does not exempt negative predicates from this.
He criticizes al-Sharif's claim as arbitrary (tahakkum).
2
Apply the principle uniformly
According to the author, any proposition with an affirmative copula (رابطة الايجاب والاثبات) requires the subject to exist, regardless of whether the predicate is positive, modified, or negated.
Thus both ma'dula al-mahmul and saliba al-mahmul require existence.
3
Conclude the universal rule
The author restates: 'The positive requires existence of the subject' is general and not restricted to specific types. Similarly, 'The negative does not require existence' remains general.
This resolves the confusion by keeping the simple negative as the only type without existential import.

اما الاول فلان معنى قولنا زيد لاكاتب هو ان اللاكاتب ثابت لزيد و كلما صدق اللاكاتب ان يقال: زيد ليس بكاتب و الا لصدق الكاتب على زيد هذا خلف.
و اما الثاني فلان معنى قولنا زيد ليس بكاتب هو ان الكتابة مسلوبة عن زيد وهذا يصدق على زيد الموجود والمعدوم بخلاف قولنا زيد لاكاتب فانه لا يصدق الا على الموجود، ضرورة ان اثبات شيء لغيره فرع وجود المثبت له سواء كان ذلك الشيء امراً وجودياً أو عدمياً.
فان قلت لم لم يلتفتوا إلى بيان النسبة بين معدولة المحمول و الموجبة المحصلة و بين السالبة المحصلة والموجبة المحصلة؟

قال المحقق الشريف: صدق الموجبة سالبة سالبة المحمول لئلا يقتضى وجود الموضوع لان حقيقتها راجعة إلى معنى السالبة ضرورة ان انتفاء شيء عن آخر يستلزم اتصاف الاخر بانتفاء ذلك الشيء عنه و بالعكس بل لا اختلاف بينها الا بالاعتبار ولاشك ان صدق السالبة لا يقتضي وجود الموضوع فهكذا ما يلازمها يعني كما ان انتفاء المحمول عن الموضوع لا يقتضى وجود الموضوع حال الانتفاء، كذلك اتصاف الموضوع بانتفاء المحمول لا يقتضى وجوده حال الاتصاف بهذا الانتفاء لانه لازم مساوقه. و فيه ان قولهم ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له قضية بديهية أولية يحكم بها بديهية العقل ولا يستثنى العقل منها الأمر السلبي، والقول بان العقل يستثنى سالبة المحمول دون معدولة المحمول تحكم. فالحق ان الموجبة مطلقا يقتضى وجود الموضوع ولا فرق فى ذلك بين معدولة المحمول و سالبة المحمول لان المقتضى لوجود الموضوع هو دخول رابطة الايجاب والاثبات و ان كان المحمول سلبياً على انه يلزم مما ذكروه ان لا يقتضى الموجبة معدولة المحمول ايضاً وجود الموضوع، فقولهم : الموجبة تقتضى وجود الموضوع باق على اطلاقه ولم يخصص بالموجبة سالبة المحمول وكذا قولهم السالبة لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع، باق على اطلاقه ولم يخصص بالسالبة سالبة المحمول (عبد الرحيم)
On assents. As for the first, because the meaning of our saying 'Zaid is not-a-writer' is that not-writer is established for Zaid, and whenever not-writer is true, it is said: 'Zaid is not a writer', otherwise 'writer' would be true of Zaid, which is absurd.
