Defining the Muhassala Proposition
Defines muhassala and distinguishes it from basita

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is muhassala (realized) by checking whether any negative particle is part of its subject or predicate, and distinguish it from basita.

Core Idea
A proposition is muhassala if its negative, if present, does not infect the subject or predicate.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by the presence and role of negation. This passage clarifies a key distinction: a proposition may contain a negative particle but still be considered 'muhassala' (realized) if the negation does not become part of either term. Understanding this classification is essential for correct syllogistic transformations, especially in legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for muhassala
Read the Arabic: 'سواء لم يكن فيها حرف سلب أصلاً او يكون ولم يكن جزء من جزء منها'. The proposition is muhassala if it has no negative particle at all OR if it has one but that particle is not part of any term (subject or predicate).
The condition is disjunctive: either no negation, or negation not in a term.
2
Understand the rationale for the name
The author says: 'لان حرف السلب لما لم يكن جزء من جزئها فكل من الطرفين وجودى محصل'. Because the negative is not part of a term, each term is a positive, realized entity (wujudī muḥaṣṣal). This is why it is called 'muhassala' — the terms are 'realized'.
3
Distinguish basita from muhassala
The commentator notes that 'muhassala' is sometimes restricted to positive propositions, and negative propositions that meet the condition are called 'basita' (simple). Both are muhassala in the broad sense, but basita emphasizes the negative's externality.
Basita literally means 'simple' — a simple negative has the negation outside the terms.
4
Apply the definition to examples
Consider: (a) 'Zaid is a writer' — no negative, so muhassala. (b) 'Zaid is not a writer' — negative present but not part of a term, so muhassala (or basita). (c) 'Zaid is not-a-writer' (i.e., the predicate is a negated term) — the negative is part of the predicate, so NOT muhassala.
This step solidifies the classification by testing boundary cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۵۹) سواء لم يكن فيها حرف سلب أصلاً او يكون ولم يكن جزء من جزء منها .
و انما سميت محصلة، لان حرف السلب لما لم يكن جزء من جزئها فكل من الطرفين وجودى محصل. وربما يختص اسم المحصلة بالموجبة ويسمى السالبة بسيطة لان حرف السلب ليست جزء من جزئها وان كانت موجودة فيها (شيخ عبد الرحيم)
(59) Whether it has no negative particle at all, or it does have one but not as a part of one of its parts. And it is only called muhassala because when the negative particle is not part of one of its parts, each of the two terms is a positive, realized thing. And perhaps the name muhassala is specific to the positive, and the negative is called basita (simple) because the negative particle is not part of one of its parts, even if it is present in it. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This short passage defines what makes a proposition 'muhassala' (a term that literally means 'realized' or 'produced'). A proposition is muhassala if it meets one of two conditions: either it contains no negative particle at all, or if it does contain one, that particle is not a part of either the subject or the predicate. In other words, the negative particle, if present, operates on the whole proposition (as in a simple negative) rather than being incorporated into a term. The reason for the name is that both terms (subject and predicate) are positive and 'realized' (muhassal) entities. The commentator notes that the term 'muhassala' is sometimes reserved for positive propositions, while negative propositions that fit the same condition (no negative in the terms) are called 'basita' (simple). This distinction clarifies that a simple negative like 'Zaid is not a writer' is considered muhassala in a broader sense or basita specifically, because its negative particle is not part of either term.

محصلة
بسيطة

The Essence, Description, and Heading of the Subject
Analyzes the subject into essence, description, and heading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the essence (dhat), description (wasf), and heading ('unwan) of the subject in a proposition and identify the three types of heading.

Core Idea
A subject's heading may be identical, partial, or external to its essence—this determines how the proposition's referents are identified.

In this passage, the author delves into the anatomy of the subject term in a proposition. He explains that the subject is more than just a word: it has an essence (what it really refers to), a description (the concept as understood), and a heading (the term used to pick out the individuals). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analyzing how propositions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects of the subject
Find where the author mentions 'dhat al-mawdu', 'wasf al-mawdu', and ''unwan'. Note that the first two are apparent ('zahiran'), while the heading is what makes the essence known.
2
Understand the heading's relationship to the essence
The heading can be identical (like 'man' for humans), a part (like 'animal'), or external/accidental (like 'sensitive walker'). The author gives examples from quantified propositions like 'every man', 'every animal', 'every sensitive walker'.
3
Connect to the five universals
The author references the earlier discussion of the five universals (al-kulliyyat al-khams). The universal, when applied to individuals, must fall into one of the three categories: identical, partial, or accidental. This directly maps to the types of heading.
4
Apply to a new example
Take a proposition from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' What is the heading? It is 'intoxicant'. Is it identical to the essence of the individuals (e.g., wine)? Or is it a quality? Reflect on whether it is part of the essence or external.
This is just an example to test understanding.

(۶۰) قوله «ای نسبة المحمول الى الموضوع ... اعلم ان القضية كما مر لابد له من جزء محكوم عليه ومن جزء محكوم به فالاول يسمى الموضوع والثاني المحمول وقد مر و ما صدق عليه الموضوع يسمى ذات الموضوع ومفهومه من حيث هو هو يسمى وصف الموضوع ، وعنوانه، أما الاولان فظاهران و اما الاخير فلانه يعرف به ذات الموضوع الذى هو المحكوم عليه في الحقيقة كما يعرف الكتاب مثلاً بعنوانه . والعنوان اما ان يكون عين حقيقة الذات أو جزئها او خارجاً عنها كقولنا كل انسان او كل حيوان او كل ماش حساس فان الحكم فى كل واحد منها حقيقة انما هو على نحو زيد و عمرو و بكر مما صدق عليه الموضوع، الا أنها قد عبر عنها تارة بالانسان الذي هو عين حقيقتها وتارة بالحيوان الذي هو جزء حقيقتها وتارة بالماشي الذي هو خارج عنها عارض لها وذلك ، لمامر من ان القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم المحصورات ولاشك ان الموضوع فيها مراد به الافراد وقد تقدم في مبحث الكليات الخمس ان الكلى اذا نسب الى ما صدق عليه من الافراد فلابد ان يكون أحد الاقسام الثلاثة. اذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : ان ذات الموضوع كما يتصف بوصفه و عنوانه كذا يتصف بوصف المحمول و يسمى الأول عقد الوضع والثانى عقد الحمل والاول تركيب تقييدى والثانى تركيب خبرى، فحصل مفهوم القضية الى عقدين: عقد الوضع و عقد الحمل والمقصود هيهنا هو بيان كيفية ذلك. واما الأول فسيأتى الى بيان كيفيته الاشارة من المحشى فى مبحث العكس المستوى فانتظر (محمد علی)
(60) His saying: 'i.e. the relation of the predicate to the subject... Know that the proposition, as has passed, must have a part that is judged about and a part that is judged by. The first is called the subject and the second the predicate, and it has passed. That which the subject is true of is called the essence of the subject, and its concept in itself is called the description of the subject and its heading. As for the first two, they are apparent; as for the last, because by it the essence of the subject, which is actually judged about, is known, just as a book is known by its title. The heading may be identical to the essence of the essence, or part of it, or external to it, like our saying 'every man', or 'every animal', or 'every sensitive walker'. For the judgment in each one of them is in reality about individuals like Zayd, Amr, and Bakr who are true of the subject, except that they have been expressed sometimes by 'man' which is identical to their essence, sometimes by 'animal' which is part of their essence, and sometimes by 'walker' which is external to them, accidental to them. This is because, as has passed, the propositions considered in the sciences are the quantified ones, and there is no doubt that the subject in them is intended for individuals, and it has preceded in the discussion of the five universals that the universal, when related to its individuals of which it is true, must be one of three divisions. If you have understood this, then know: the essence of the subject is characterized by its description and its heading, similarly it is characterized by the description of the predicate. The first is called 'the contract of position' and the second 'the contract of predication'; the first is a restrictive composition and the second a predicative composition. Thus the concept of the proposition consists of two contracts: the contract of position and the contract of predication. The purpose here is to explain the modality of that. As for the first, an indication of its modality will come from the commentator in the discussion of the simple conversion, so wait. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the internal structure of a proposition. The author begins by noting that every proposition has two essential parts: that which is judged about (the subject) and that which is judged by (the predicate). The subject is further analyzed: the entity that the subject term refers to (the 'essence' of the subject, dhat al-mawdu') is distinct from the concept or description (wasf) and the heading ('unwan) by which it is known. The heading can be identical to the essence (like 'man' for a human), a part of the essence (like 'animal'), or something external and accidental (like 'sensitive walker'). This is tied to the earlier discussion of the five universals and quantified propositions, where the subject is intended for individuals. Then the author introduces two 'contracts' (aqd): the 'contract of position' (aqd al-wad'), which is the restrictive composition that identifies the subject via its description or heading, and the 'contract of predication' (aqd al-haml), which is the predicative composition that links the subject to the predicate. The former is likened to a restrictive (taqyidi) composition, the latter to a predicative (khabari) composition. The author notes that the full concept of a proposition encompasses both contracts, and the present discussion focuses on the second contract; detailed explanation of the first contract is deferred to the section on simple conversion ('aks mustawi).

وصف الموضوع
عنوان

A) Identical
B) Partial
C) External
D) Contradictory

Key Takeaway
The subject in a proposition is not a simple term; it has an essence, a description, and a heading, and the heading can be identical, partial, or external to the essence.

The Contract of Position and Contract of Predication
Defines the two contracts of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the contract of position (aqd al-wad') and contract of predication (aqd al-haml) and distinguish between restrictive and predicative composition.

Core Idea
A proposition comprises two contracts: one that fixes the subject via its heading, and one that predicates something of it.

Having understood the three aspects of the subject, the author now introduces two fundamental 'contracts' that constitute a proposition. The first, the contract of position, is a restrictive composition that characterizes the subject by its description or heading. The second, the contract of predication, is a predicative composition that links the predicate to the subject. Together, these form the complete propositional structure.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two contracts in the text
The author explicitly names them: 'aqd al-wad' and 'aqd al-haml. Note that he calls the first a restrictive composition (tarkib taqyidi) and the second a predicative composition (tarkib khabari).
2
Understand the function of each contract
The contract of position 'positions' the subject by identifying which individuals are meant (via the heading). The contract of predication makes the judgment that the predicate applies to those individuals.
3
Connect to the earlier subject analysis
The contract of position relies on the heading ('unwan) of the subject. Without a proper heading, the subject is not fixed, and the proposition would be ambiguous.
4
Recognize the deferred discussion
The author notes that a detailed explanation of the contract of position will be given later in the section on simple conversion ('aks mustawi). This signals that the concept is foundational for later logical operations.

The Matter of a Proposition and Its Truth Condition
Defines matter and truth condition for modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the 'matter' of a proposition and determine whether a modal proposition is true or false by comparing its expressed modality to its matter.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth depends on whether its expressed modality matches the real modal property of the relation in reality.

In modal logic, the truth of a proposition is not merely about the fit between subject and predicate, but about whether the modality (necessity, possibility, impossibility) matches the actual state of affairs. This passage defines that real modal property as the 'matter' (madda) and gives the condition for truth: the expressed modality must align with the matter. Understanding this is key to evaluating modal statements in Islamic sciences like kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'madda' (مادة), 'jiha' (جهة), and 'kayfiyya nafs al-amriyya' (كيفية نفس الأمرية) in the Arabic. Understand that 'madda' is the real modal property, 'jiha' is the expressed modality, and 'kayfiyya nafs al-amriyya' is the actual modal quality in reality.
2
Distinguish ancients from later scholars
Notice that the passage contrasts the view of the ancients (al-qudama') and the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun). For the later scholars, matter applies to any relation (affirmative or negative) and any modality. For the ancients, it applies only to affirmative relations and only to the three modalities: necessity, possibility, impossibility.
This shift shows how the concept was generalized.
3
Understand why 'madda' is also called 'unsur'
The passage explains that matter is called 'madda' because the proposition cannot exist without it, and for the same reason it is called 'unsur' (element). This emphasizes that matter is an essential component of a modal proposition.
Other names: 'munawwa'a' (classified) and 'ruba'iyya' (quadripartite) refer to proposition types containing a modality.
4
Apply the truth condition
Read the truth condition in gloss (66): a modal proposition is true (sadiqa) when the expressed modality (jiha) matches the real modal property (matter). If they do not match, it is false (kadhiba). The example 'Man is an animal by necessity' is true because in reality the relation of animality to man is necessary.
This condition is crucial for evaluating modal claims in Islamic arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۱) قوله: «تسمى مادة القضية»: هذه العبارة بعد تعميم النسبة الى الايجاب و السلب والكيفية الى الضرورة و الدوام وغيرهما صريحة فى ان مادة القضية هى الكيفية النفس الامرية مطلقا سواء كانت القضية سالبة او موجبة وسواء كانت هى الوجوب او الامكان اوالامتناع أو غيرها و هذا عند المتأخرين منهم و أما عند القدماء فالمادة ليست كيفية كل نسبة بل كيفية النسبة الايجابية ولا كل كيفية نسبة ايجابية فى نفس الامر بل كيفية النسبة الايجابيه فى نفس الأمر بالوجوب او الامكان او الامتناع و هي لا تختلف بايجاب القضية وسلبها. ثم انما سميت تلك الكيفية مادة لانها يمتنع وجود القضية بدونها و لذلك ايضاً تسمى عنصراً. (میرزا محمد علی)

(۶۲) لاشتمالها على الجهة و تسمى ايضاً «منوعة» لاشتمالها على النوع و «رباعية» ايضاً لاشتمالها على اربعة اجزاء غالباً. (ميرزا محمد على)

(۶۳) لعدم تقييدها بالجهة وتسمى «مهملة» ايضاً لاهمال الجهة فيها. (عبد الرحيم)

(۶۵) لانها جهة ينتهى اليها القضية ولا يزيد عليها شيء، هكذا وجدت في حاشية بعض النسخ. (شیخ عبد الرحيم)

(۶۶) قوله: «فان طابقت الجهة المادة»: اى فان طابقت الجهة الدالة على الكيفية المصرح بها في القضية الموجهة باعتبار مدلولها الكيفية النفس الامرية الواقعية التى هى مادة القضية، صدقت القضية ای تسمى «صادقة» كقولنا الانسان حيوان بالضرورة فان نسبة الحيوة إلى الانسان في نفس الأمر مع قطع النظر عن مدلول اللفظ مكيفة بكيفية الضرورة، والجهة اعنى الضرورة مطابقة لها، والا اى و لم تطابق الجهة المادة و الكيفية النفس الامرية كذبت اى تسمى القضية «كاذبة» كقولنا : «كل
(61) His saying: 'It is called the matter of the proposition': This expression, after generalizing the relation to affirmation and negation and the modality to necessity, perpetuity, and others, is explicit that the matter of the proposition is the modal property in reality (al-kayfiyya al-nafs al-amriyya) absolutely, whether the proposition is negative or positive, and whether it is necessity, possibility, impossibility, or others. This is according to the later scholars among them. As for the ancients, the matter is not the modality of every relation, but rather the modality of the affirmative relation, and not every modality of an affirmative relation in reality, but rather the modality of the affirmative relation in reality by necessity, possibility, or impossibility; and it does not vary with the affirmation or negation of the proposition. Then that modality is called 'matter' because the proposition cannot exist without it, and for that reason it is also called 'element' (unsur). (Mirza Muhammad Ali)

(62) Because it contains the modality, and it is also called 'classified' (munawwa'a) because it contains the kind, and 'quadripartite' (ruba'iyya) because it mostly contains four parts. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)

(63) Because it is not restricted by the modality, and it is also called 'neglected' (muhmala) because the modality is neglected in it. (Abd al-Rahim)

(64) i.e., upon the explicitly stated modality that is claimed to be the modal property in reality absolutely, not upon the real modal property in reality as is apparent to the contemplator. (Muhammad Ali)

(65) Because it is a modality at which the proposition ends and nothing is added to it. This is found so in the gloss of some copies. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim)

(66) His saying: 'If the modality matches the matter': i.e., if the modality indicating the explicitly stated modality in the modal proposition, in consideration of its denotation, matches the real modal property in reality which is the matter of the proposition, then the proposition is true, i.e., it is called 'true' like our saying 'Man is an animal by necessity', for the relation of animality to man in reality, disregarding the denotation of the word, is characterized by the modality of necessity, and the modality, i.e. necessity, matches it. Otherwise, i.e. if the modality does not match the matter and the real modal property in reality, then it is false, i.e., the proposition is called 'false', like our saying: 'Every...'
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage collects several glosses discussing the concept of 'matter' (madda) of a proposition, its alternative names, and the truth condition for modal propositions. The first gloss (61) explains that the matter is the real modal property (al-kayfiyya al-nafs al-amriyya) of the relation between subject and predicate, regardless of whether the proposition is affirmative or negative, and regardless of the specific modality (necessity, possibility, impossibility, etc.). This is the view of the later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun). In contrast, the ancients (al-qudama') defined matter only for affirmative relations and specifically for the three modalities: necessity, possibility, and impossibility. The term 'matter' is used because the proposition cannot exist without it; hence it is also called 'element' (unsur). The second gloss (62) gives additional names: 'munawwa'a' (because it contains the kind or species of modality), and 'ruba'iyya' (because it often consists of four parts: subject, predicate, relation, and modality). The third gloss (63) refers to non-modal (absolute) propositions, which are called 'muhmala' (neglected) because the modality is neglected or omitted. The fourth gloss (64) clarifies that when speaking of the explicitly stated modality in a modal proposition, it is the claimed real modality, not necessarily the actual real modality. The fifth gloss (65) provides an etymology: 'madda' (matter) is so called because it is the final modality of the proposition beyond which nothing is added. The sixth gloss (66) explains the truth condition: a modal proposition is true (sadiqa) when the modality explicitly expressed (al-jiha) matches the real modal property in reality (the matter); otherwise, it is false (kadhiba). An example is given: 'Man is an animal by necessity' is true because in reality the relation of animality to man is necessary, so the modality 'necessity' matches the matter. The end of the gloss is cut off, but it is clear.

جهة
كيفية نفس الأمرية
صادقة
كاذبة
عنصر
منوعة
مهملة

A) The expressed modality must be necessity

C) The proposition must be affirmative
D) The proposition must be quantified

Key Takeaway
The truth of a modal proposition depends on whether its expressed modality matches the real modal property (matter) of the relation in reality.

The Subject-Posit ('Aql al-Wad) in Propositions
Author explains the two posits that constitute the meaning of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject-posit ('aql al-wad') in a proposition from Islamic logic and explain its role as a restrictive composition.

Core Idea
Subject-posit ties the subject to its own description, forming a restrictive composition.

In this passage, the author introduces the first component of a proposition: 'aql al-wad', or the subject-posit. This is the conceptual structure that links the subject to its own description or title, creating a restrictive composition. Understanding this helps you see how definitions are built in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the subject-posit
In a proposition, the subject-posit is the composition that connects the subject to its own essence or description (not the predicate). For example, in 'Human is rational animal', the subject-posit is the concept 'human' with its essential description 'rational animal'.
2
Verify it is restrictive
The composition is restrictive (taqyidi) because it limits the subject to that specific description. It does not assert anything beyond the subject's own nature.
3
Distinguish from predicate-posit
The predicate-posit ('aql al-haml) is different: it links the subject to an external predicate. The subject-posit is internal to the subject's definition.
This distinction is key to analyzing propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان ذات الموضوع كما يتصف بوصفه و عنوانه كذا يتصف بوصف المحمول و يسمى الأول عقد الوضع والثانى عقد الحمل والاول تركيب تقييدى والثانى تركيب خبرى، فحصل مفهوم القضية الى عقدين: عقد الوضع و عقد الحمل والمقصود هيهنا هو بيان كيفية ذلك. واما الأول فسيأتى الى بيان كيفيته الاشارة من المحشى فى مبحث العكس المستوى فانتظر (محمد علی)
That the subject-essence is as described by its description and its title, so it is described by the description of the predicate. The first is called the position-posit and the second the predicate-posit; the first is a restrictive composition and the second a predicative composition. So the concept of the proposition becomes two posits: the position-posit and the predicate-posit, and the purpose here is to explain the modality of that. As for the first, the reference from the glossator will come in the chapter on simple conversion, so wait. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the two conceptual components of a proposition: the subject-posit and the predicate-posit. The author explains that just as the subject-essence is qualified by its own description and title, it is also qualified by the predicate's description. The first relation (subject with its description) is called 'aql al-wad' (position-posit) and it is a restrictive composition; the second (subject with predicate) is called 'aql al-haml' (predicate-posit) and it is a predicative composition. The proposition's meaning results from these two posits. The passage then notes that the detailed explanation of the first posit will come later in the chapter on simple conversion, indicating the systematic structure of the work. This distinction is crucial for understanding the internal structure of propositions in logic, particularly in how definitions and predications are formed.

The Predicate-Posit ('Aql al-Haml) in Propositions
Author explains the two posits that constitute the meaning of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the predicate-posit ('aql al-haml') in a proposition from Islamic logic and explain its role as a predicative composition.

Core Idea
Predicate-posit links subject to predicate, forming a predicative composition.

The second component of a proposition is 'aql al-haml', the predicate-posit. This is the conceptual structure that connects the subject to the predicate, forming a predicative composition. Understanding this is key to analyzing statements in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Locate the predicate-posit
The predicate-posit is the composition that connects the subject to the predicate. For example, in 'The world is created', the predicate-posit links 'world' (subject) with 'created' (predicate).
2
Verify it is predicative
The composition is predicative (khabari) because it asserts something about the subject, rather than restricting it to its own essence.
3
Distinguish from subject-posit
The subject-posit ('aql al-wad) is internal to the subject's definition; the predicate-posit is external, linking the subject to something else.
This distinction helps avoid confusion in logical analysis.

The Matter (Madda) of the Proposition: Definition and Synonyms
Author defines the term 'matter' of a proposition and gives its synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the matter (madda) of a proposition, distinguish between the earlier and later views, and identify its synonyms.

Core Idea
The matter is the essential modal quality that makes a proposition what it is.

Every proposition has a core modal quality that determines its truth condition in reality. This quality, called 'matter' (madda), is what logicians analyze to understand necessity, possibility, and impossibility. In this passage, the glossator explains the definition of madda and its various names, highlighting a key historical shift in how it was understood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'madda al-qadiyya' (مادة القضية). Note that it is a technical term for the real modal quality of the relation between subject and predicate.
2
Extract the core definition according to later logicians
Later logicians say the matter is the real modal quality (kayfiyya nafsi amriyya) of the relation, whether affirmative or negative, and regardless of the specific mode (obligatoriness, possibility, impossibility).
This is a broader definition than the earlier one.
3
Understand the earlier view
Earlier logicians restricted matter to the quality of the affirmative relation only, not the negative. They also required it to be in reality (nafs al-amr) with a specific mode.
This distinction is crucial for historical development.
4
Recognize the rationale for the term 'matter' and its synonyms
It is called 'matter' because the proposition cannot exist without it, hence also 'element' (unsur). Additional synonyms: 'diversifier' (munawwi'a) because it contains the type, 'quadripartite' (ruba'iyya) because it often has four parts, and 'neglected' (muhmala) when modality is absent.
Each synonym highlights a different aspect of the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۱) قوله: «تسمى مادة القضية»: هذه العبارة بعد تعميم النسبة الى الايجاب و السلب والكيفية الى الضرورة و الدوام وغيرهما صريحة فى ان مادة القضية هى الكيفية النفس الامرية مطلقا سواء كانت القضية سالبة او موجبة وسواء كانت هى الوجوب او الامكان اوالامتناع أو غيرها و هذا عند المتأخرين منهم و أما عند القدماء فالمادة ليست كيفية كل نسبة بل كيفية النسبة الايجابية ولا كل كيفية نسبة ايجابية فى نفس الامر بل كيفية النسبة الايجابيه فى نفس الأمر بالوجوب او الامكان او الامتناع و هي لا تختلف بايجاب القضية وسلبها. ثم انما سميت تلك الكيفية مادة لانها يمتنع وجود القضية بدونها و لذلك ايضاً تسمى عنصراً. (میرزا محمد علی) (۶۲) لاشتمالها على الجهة و تسمى ايضاً «منوعة» لاشتمالها على النوع و «رباعية» ايضاً لاشتمالها على اربعة اجزاء غالباً. (ميرزا محمد على) (۶۳) لعدم تقييدها بالجهة وتسمى «مهملة» ايضاً لاهمال الجهة فيها. (عبد الرحيم)
(61) His saying: 'It is called the matter of the proposition': This statement, after generalizing the relation to affirmation and negation and the modality to necessity, perpetuity, and others, is explicit that the matter of the proposition is the real modal quality absolutely, whether the proposition is negative or affirmative and whether it is obligatoriness, possibility, impossibility, or others. And this is according to the later scholars among them. As for the earlier scholars, the matter is not the quality of every relation, but the quality of the affirmative relation, and not every quality of an affirmative relation in reality, but the quality of the affirmative relation in reality with obligatoriness, possibility, or impossibility, and it does not differ by affirmation or negation of the proposition. Then that quality is called matter because the proposition cannot exist without it, and for that reason it is also called element. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (62) Because it contains the modality, and it is also called 'diversifier' because it contains the type, and 'quadripartite' because it contains four parts mostly. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (63) Because it is not restricted by modality, and it is also called 'neglected' because the modality is neglected in it. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'matter' (madda) of a proposition and provides its synonyms. The glossator explains that, according to later logicians, the matter is the real modal quality (kayfiyya nafsi amriyya) of the relation between subject and predicate, regardless of whether the proposition is affirmative or negative, and regardless of the specific mode (obligatoriness, possibility, impossibility, etc.). According to the earlier logicians, however, the matter is only the quality of the affirmative relation in reality, not the negative. The term 'matter' is used because the proposition cannot exist without this quality; hence it is also called 'element' (unsur). Additional synonyms are given: it is called 'diversifier' (munawwi'a) because it includes the type, and 'quadripartite' (ruba'iyya) because it often contains four parts (subject, predicate, modality, quantifier). When the proposition lacks modality, it is called 'neglected' (muhmala). This classification helps in understanding the components of propositions in modal logic.

الكيفية النفس الامرية
عنصر

رباعية
مهملة

Key Takeaway

Truth Condition for Modal Propositions
Author defines the condition for a modal proposition's truth as correspondence between modality and real matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a modal proposition is true or false by checking if the expressed modality matches the real modal quality (madda).

Core Idea
A modal proposition's truth depends on its modality matching reality, not the speaker's belief.

In logic, modal propositions include words like 'necessarily' or 'possibly'. But when is such a proposition true? The author explains that truth requires the modality to correspond to the real state of affairs. This lesson unpacks that condition and solves a potential confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality (jiha)
Look for the word or expression indicating the mode (e.g., 'necessarily', 'possibly') in the proposition. This is the expressed modality.
The modality may be a word like 'ḍarūra' (necessity) or 'imkān' (possibility).
2
Determine the real modal quality (madda)
Analyze the actual relation between subject and predicate in reality, disregarding the speaker's belief. Ask: in reality, is the relation necessary, possible, or impossible?
The madda is the real mode of the relation, not the speaker's assertion.
3
Compare modality and matter
If the expressed modality matches the real modal quality, the proposition is true. If they differ, the proposition is false.
Example: 'Man is necessarily animal' is true because in reality the relation is necessary. 'Man is necessarily stone' is false because in reality it is impossible.
4
Recognize the distinction between belief and reality
The modality indicates the speaker's belief about the real quality, not necessarily the actual real quality. Even false propositions have a modality that expresses what the speaker claims.
This resolves the objection: the modality indicates a broader concept (belief) that may not coincide with actual reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۶) قوله: «فان طابقت الجهة المادة»: اى فان طابقت الجهة الدانة على الكيفية المصرح بها في القضية الموجهة باعتبار مدلولها الكيفية النفس الامرية الواقعية التى هى مادة القضية، صدقت القضية ای تسمى «صادقة» كقولنا الانسان حيوان بالضرورة فان نسبة الحيوة إلى الانسان في نفس الأمر مع قطع النظر عن مدلول اللفظ مكيفة بكيفية الضرورة، والجهة اعنى الضرورة مطابقة لها، والا اى و لم تطابق الجهة المادة و الكيفية النفس الامرية كذبت اى تسمى القضية «كاذبة» كقولنا : «كل انسان حجر بالضرورة فان نسبة الحجرية الى الانسان في نفس الامر والواقع انما هي بالامتناع والجهة وهي الضرورة غير مطابقة له هذا، وبما عرفت من ان الجهة هى اللفظ او الصورة العقلية الدالان على الكيفية النفس الامرية بحسب اعتقاد المتكلم المفاد من ظاهر كلامه مطلقا سواء كانت مطابقة للكيفية النفس الامرية الواقعية ام لا، ظهر اندفاع ما ربما يتوهم هنا من ان الجهة اذا لم تطابق المادة التي هي الكيفية النفس الامرية وخالفتها لم تكن دالة على الكيفية النفس الامرية و اللازم باطل، ضرورة انها عبارة عما تدل على الكيفية النفس الامرية و ذلك لان الكيفية النفس الامرية التي تدل عليها الجهة غير الكيفية النفس الامرية التي هي مادة القضية فان الأولى كما ذكر اعم من الثانية و الايراد انما يلزم على تقدير الاتحاد لا التغاير فما يوهم من عبائر الجماعة كعبارة المحشى حيث قال: «واللفظ الدال عليها ...» ان الكيفيتين متحدتان، فلابد ان يؤل بالاستخدام وغيره مما يمكن فى المقام ويدل على المرام من غير عذر ولا كلام (میرزا محمد علی) (۶۷) اشارة الى ان مراد المصنف بقوله بضرورة النسبة» ما يتناول الوقوع واللا وقوع فلا يرد ان تعريف المصنف غير شامل للسوالب (میرزا محمد علی)
(66) His saying: 'So if the modality corresponds to the matter': i.e., if the modality, which indicates the quality expressed in the modal proposition in terms of its meaning, corresponds to the real modal quality that is the matter of the proposition, the proposition is true, i.e., called 'true', as in our saying 'Man is necessarily animal', because the relation of life to man in reality, disregarding the meaning of the word, is qualified by the quality of necessity, and the modality, i.e., necessity, corresponds to it. Otherwise, i.e., if the modality does not correspond to the matter and the real modal quality, the proposition is false, i.e., called 'false', as in our saying 'Every man is necessarily stone', because the relation of stoniness to man in reality is only by impossibility, and the modality, i.e., necessity, does not correspond to it. And by what you have known—that the modality is the word or mental form that indicates the real modal quality according to the belief of the speaker, derived from the apparent meaning of his speech, regardless of whether it corresponds to the actual real modal quality or not—the apparent objection is refuted: that if the modality does not correspond to the matter which is the real modal quality and differs from it, then it would not indicate the real modal quality, and the consequent is false. This is because the real modal quality indicated by the modality is other than the real modal quality that is the matter of the proposition; the former is, as mentioned, more general than the latter. The objection would only follow on the assumption of unity, not difference. So what might be suggested by the expressions of the group, like the expression of the glossator where he said 'and the word indicating it...', that the two qualities are identical, must be interpreted by usage (istikhdam) or something possible in the context that indicates the intent, without excuse or discussion. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (67) An indication that the author's intent by his saying 'with necessity of the relation' includes occurrence and non-occurrence, so it is not objected that the author's definition does not include negatives. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage explains the truth condition for modal propositions: a modal proposition is true when its expressed modality (al-jiha) matches the real modal quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafs al-amriyya al-waqi'iyya) which is the matter of the proposition. Two examples illustrate: 'Man is necessarily animal' is true because in reality the relation is necessary; 'Every man is necessarily stone' is false because in reality the relation is impossible. The glossator then addresses a potential objection: if the modality does not match the real matter, how can it be said to indicate the real modal quality? The answer is that the modality indicates a belief (i'tiqad) about the real quality, not the actual real quality itself. Thus, the two are distinct: the modality indicates a broader concept that may or may not coincide with reality. This resolves the apparent contradiction. Finally, a note clarifies that the author's phrase 'with necessity of the relation' is intended to include both positive and negative relations, so the definition covers negative modal propositions as well.

المادة (القضية)

Key Takeaway
A modal proposition is true only when its expressed modality matches the real mode of the relation in actual reality.

Divisions of Necessity: Absolute and Conditional
Author discusses the divisions of necessity, focusing on absolute and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute necessity and general conditional necessity, and explain why temporal and diffusive necessities are omitted in basic logical discussions.

Core Idea
Necessity is divided into absolute and conditional for simplicity in logical analysis.

In this passage, the author explains that some logicians reduce the divisions of necessity to two main types: absolute necessity (al-darura al-mutlaqa) and general conditional necessity (al-mashruta al-'amma). They leave aside absolute temporal and absolute diffusive, because these are not used in discussions of contradiction, conversion, and syllogisms. Understanding this division is crucial for working with modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions of necessity
Locate where the author states that some divided necessity into 'absolute necessity' and 'general conditional necessity'. Note that these are the only two considered primary.
The text says: 'Some have reduced the divisions of necessity to two: absolute necessity and general conditional necessity.'
2
Understand why temporal and diffusive are omitted
The author notes that 'absolute temporal' and 'absolute diffusive' are set aside because they are not used in the core topics of contradiction, conversion, and syllogisms. They are considered only in compound propositions.
The phrase 'they did not consider them in the discussions of contradiction, conversions, and syllogisms' is key.
3
Grasp the relative nature of absolute necessity
The term 'absolute' is relative to the two restrictions of description and time. It does not mean unconditional in every sense; it is still restricted in some ways (e.g., by the subject's existence).
The glossator clarifies: 'its being called absolute is only in relation to the remaining mentioned divisions of necessity.'
4
Recognise the whole-part naming convention
Calling a proposition 'necessary' is like naming the whole after a part, because necessity is only one aspect of the proposition's modality.
The phrase 'naming the whole by the name of a part' is used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۸) قد حضر بعضهم اقسام الضرورة على ثنتين: «الضرورة المطلقة» و «المشروطة العامة» و ترك «الوقتية المطلقة» و «المنتشرة المطلقة» انه ذكر في المركبات الوقتية و المنتشرة و هما الوقتية و مع المنتشرة المطلقتان المقيدتان باللادوام الذاتى و لعل وجهه انهم لم يعتبروهما في مباحث التناقض و العكوس والأقيسة بخلاف باقى البسايط وسيأتى من المحشى اعتراف بذلك (میرزا محمد علی) (۶۹) فيكون تسميتها بالضرورية لكونه منسوبة الى الضرورة نسبة الكل الى الجزء (محمد على) (۷۰) قوله : و عدم تقييد الضرورة بالوصف او الوقت» اشارة الى وجه تسميتها بالمطلقة على طريقة اللف المرتب. ثم لا يخفى ان الاطلاق فى الحقيقة وصف الضرورة كما هو ظاهر قتسميتها بالمطلقة من قبيل تسمية الكل باسم الجزء. ولا يخفى أيضاً ان اطلاق الضرورة انما هو بالنسبة إلى القيدين المذكورين اعنى: الوصف والوقت فان تسميتها بالمطلقة انما هي بالنسبة الى بواق اقسام الضرورة المذكورة لا مطلقا حتى يقال انها أيضاً مقيدة بوقت وجود الموضوع. (میرزا محمد علی) (۷۱) الوصف العنواني بياء النسبة يعنى به مفهوم الموضوع بان يراد بالوصف معناه الاصطلاحي و بالعنوان معناه اللغوى اعنى ما يستدل به على الشيء و النسبة من قبيل نسبة الجزئى الى الكلى او بان يراد من العنوان معناه الاصطلاحى و بالوصف اما معناه الاصطلاحي ايضاً فالنسبة للمبالغة كقولهم: يقربهم هلميات...» او من باب نسبة المعنى الى اللفظ الى الوصف الذي هو صاحب هذا اللفظ ، او معناه اللغوى فالنسبة من قبيل نسبة الكلى الى الجزئى. ثم اعلم ان المشروطة قد تطلق و يراد بها القضية التي حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة بشرط الوصف أى: يكون له مدخل في الضرورة كالمثال الذى ذكره المحشي وقد تطلق و يراد بها القضية التي حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة مادام الوصف اى فى جميع اوقات اتصاف الذات بالوصف العنواني سواء كان ذلك
(68) Some have reduced the divisions of necessity to two: 'absolute necessity' and 'general conditional necessity', leaving aside 'absolute temporal' and 'absolute diffusive', because they mentioned in the temporal and diffusive compounds, and both temporal and diffusive are the two absolute ones restricted by essential non-permanence. Perhaps the reason is that they did not consider them in the discussions of contradiction, conversions, and syllogisms, unlike the rest of the simple ones. The glossator will later acknowledge that. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (69) So it is called 'necessary' because it is related to necessity as the whole to the part. (Muhammad Ali) (70) His saying: 'And not restricting necessity by description or time' indicates the reason for calling it 'absolute' in the manner of intertwined arrangement. Then it is not hidden that absoluteness in reality is a description of necessity, as is clear, and its being called 'absolute' is a case of naming the whole by the name of a part. It is also not hidden that the absoluteness of necessity is only in relation to the two mentioned restrictions: description and time, because its being called 'absolute' is only in relation to the remaining mentioned divisions of necessity, not absolutely such that it is said that it is also restricted by the time of existence of the subject. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (71) The descriptive title with the relative ya' means the concept of the subject, such that by 'description' is meant its technical meaning and by 'title' its linguistic meaning, i.e., what is used to indicate the thing, and the relation is like the relation of a particular to a universal; or by 'title' is meant its technical meaning and by 'description' also its technical meaning, then the relation is for hyperbole, like their saying: '...' or from the باب of relating meaning to word, to the description which is the owner of this word, or its linguistic meaning, then the relation is like the relation of a universal to a particular. Then know that the conditional (mashruta) may be used to refer to the proposition in which the necessity of the relation is judged on condition of the description, i.e., the description has an entry in the necessity, like the example mentioned by the glossator; and it may be used to refer to the proposition in which the necessity of the relation is judged as long as the description exists, i.e., in all times of the subject's being qualified by the descriptive title, whether that...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of modal propositions concerning necessity. Some scholars reduce the types of necessity to two: absolute necessity (darura mutlaqa) and general conditional necessity (mashruta 'amma), omitting absolute temporal and absolute diffusive because these two are not used in discussions of contradiction, conversion, and syllogisms. The author notes that calling a proposition 'necessary' is like naming the whole after a part. The term 'absolute' (mutlaqa) indicates that necessity is not restricted by description or time, but this absoluteness is only relative to the two restrictions of description and time, not absolute in every sense. The passage then explains the term 'descriptive title' (al-wasf al-'unwani) in detail, distinguishing between technical and linguistic meanings of 'description' and 'title'. Finally, it clarifies the two senses of 'conditional' (mashruta): one where the description is a condition for the necessity (i.e., the necessity is conditioned by the description), and another where the necessity persists as long as the description exists (durational). This distinction is critical for understanding temporal vs. conditional necessity in logic.

Key Terms
الضرورة المطلقة
المشروطة العامة

D) Because they are only used in compound propositions

Key Takeaway
The division of necessity into absolute and conditional is a practical simplification, where 'absolute' is relative to the other divisions, and certain types are omitted due to limited use in core logical operations.

The Two Interpretations of Conditional Necessity (Mashruta)
Author explains the two meanings of 'mashruta' (conditional necessity) and the nuances of the descriptive title.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the two meanings of 'mashruta' (conditioned necessity and durational necessity) and apply them to modal propositions.

Core Idea
Conditional necessity has two faces: necessity conditioned by a description, and necessity persisting as long as the description holds.

The passage delves into the term 'mashruta' (conditional necessity) and clarifies that it can be understood in two distinct ways: either the description is a condition for the necessity itself, or the necessity persists as long as the description exists. This nuance is vital for correctly interpreting modal statements in Islamic logic, especially when analysing propositions like 'Every writer necessarily moves his fingers while writing'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two senses of mashruta
Identify that the author distinguishes between 'condition of necessity' (the description is a condition for the necessity) and 'duration of necessity' (the necessity holds as long as the description persists). These are two different interpretations.
The text says: 'the conditional (mashruta) may be used to refer to the proposition in which the necessity of the relation is judged on condition of the description... and it may be used to refer to the proposition in which the necessity of the relation is judged as long as the description exists.'
2
Examine the role of the descriptive title (al-wasf al-'unwani)
The author explains the phrase 'descriptive title' in various ways, linking it to the concept of the subject. This analysis helps to clarify how the description relates to the necessity.
The passage gives multiple ways to understand the relation between 'description' and 'title', indicating a nuanced discussion.
3
Apply the distinction to a logical proposition
Take a concrete example, such as 'Every writer necessarily moves his fingers while writing'. Determine which sense of mashruta applies: is the necessity conditioned on being a writer, or does it last as long as one is a writer?
Consider the two interpretations: condition (if the description is removed, necessity vanishes) vs. duration (as long as description exists, necessity holds).

Clarifying Absolute Temporal and Absolute Dispersed Propositions
Author clarifies meanings of المطلقة الوقتية and المطلقة المنتشرة.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish المطلقة الوقتية from المطلقة المنتشرة and explain why they are not truly absolute.

Core Idea
Modal propositions have layers; 'absolute' here means 'less restricted', not unrestricted.

In modal logic, propositions can be qualified by time. This lesson unpacks two specific types: المطلقة الوقتية and المطلقة المنتشرة. The commentator warns that despite the label 'absolute', these are actually temporally qualified and distinct from the fully unrestricted absolute. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when analyzing modal claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate the definitions in the Arabic. The first (المطلقة الوقتية) rules actuality at a specific time; the second (المطلقة المنتشرة) rules actuality at an unspecified time.
2
Understand the temporal qualification
Both propositions are temporally qualified but differ in specificity of time. Note the contrast between 'specific' and 'unspecified'.
3
Grasp the 'absolute' qualifier
The term 'absolute' (مطلقة) here is relative: these propositions are absolute only compared to the composite forms that include non-perpetuity (اللادوام). They are not unrestricted universals.
4
Distinguish from truly absolute
Recognize that these are still modal propositions, not the fully unrestricted absolute (المطلقة بلا قيد). The refinement shows they are a distinct category within the modal system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فتسمى ح وقتية مطلقة»: لا يذهب عليك : ان ما يترأى في كلمات بعضهم من المطلقة الوقتية والمطلقة المنتشرة ليس المراد بهما الوقتية المطلقة و المنتشرة المطلقة بل هما من القضاياء المعتبرة عندهم والاولى هى التي حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة فى وقت معين والثانية هي التي حكم فيها يفعليتها في وقت غير معين (میرزا محمد علی). یعنی ان اطلاقها بالنسبة الى الوقتية المقيدة باللادوام التى هى من المركبات والا فهي مقيدة بالوقت وكذلك المنتشرة المطلقة فان اطلاقها ايضاً بالنسبة الى المنتشرة المقيدة باللادوام التي هي من المركبات وان كانت مقيدة بالوقت ايضاً فاستيقظ. (میرزا محمد علی)
His saying: 'So it is called absolute temporal.' – Let it not escape you that what appears in the words of some scholars regarding the absolute temporal and the absolute dispersed – what is meant by them is not the absolute temporal and the absolute dispersed, but rather they are among the considered propositions in their view. The first is that in which actuality of the relation is ruled in a specific time, and the second is that in which actuality is ruled in an unspecified time. (Mirza Muhammad Ali). Meaning that their absoluteness is relative to the temporal [limited] by non-perpetuity, which is among the composite [propositions]; otherwise, they are qualified by time. Similarly, the absolute dispersed – its absoluteness is relative to the dispersed qualified by non-perpetuity, which is among the composites, even though they are also qualified by time. So be alert. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)

Commentary
This passage clarifies two types of modal propositions: المطلقة الوقتية (absolute temporal) and المطلقة المنتشرة (absolute dispersed). The commentator warns that despite the term 'absolute' (مطلقة), these are not unrestricted universals but are specific modal types that are considered among the standard propositions. The first (وقتية مطلقة) asserts the actual occurrence of the relation at a specific time. The second (منتشرة مطلقة) asserts the actual occurrence at an unspecified time. The second commentator further explains that their 'absoluteness' is relative to the corresponding propositions that are qualified by non-perpetuity (اللادوام), which are composites. Both are still temporally qualified. The point is that these are not truly absolute; they are just less restrictive than the ones limited by non-perpetuity.

Distinguishing Temporal and Extended Absolute Propositions
Author clarifies two types of absolute propositions: temporal and extended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between temporal absolute and extended absolute propositions and explain their relative nature.

Core Idea
In Islamic logic, 'absolute' often means relative, not unrestricted.

This passage from a hashshiyah clarifies two types of absolute propositions: temporal and extended. While they are called 'absolute,' they are actually restricted by time, but considered absolute in relation to the more restricted forms that include non-permanence. Let's unpack the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate the terms 'temporal absolute' and 'extended absolute' in the passage. Note that they are called 'absolute temporal' and 'absolute extended' but are not absolutely unrestricted.
2
Determine the temporal condition of each
The temporal absolute is judged true at a specified time. The extended absolute is judged true at an unspecified time. This is the key differentia.
3
Understand the role of non-permanence
The author warns that these are called 'absolute' only in relation to propositions restricted by non-permanence (lā dawām). Without this relation, they are actually time-bound.
4
Grasp the relative meaning of 'absolute'
Realize that in this context, 'absolute' does not mean fully unrestricted but only less restricted than the composites that explicitly include non-permanence.
This relativization is a common theme in Islamic logic.

The temporal absolute is a proposition that asserts something is true at a specific time (e.g., 'Zayd is standing at noon'), while the extended absolute asserts truth at an indefinite time (e.g., 'Zayd is standing sometimes'). Both are actually restricted by time, but they are considered absolute because they are not further qualified by non-permanence. The author emphasizes that their absoluteness is relative to the more restricted forms.

The Relationship between Necessity and Perpetuity
Author establishes the absolute generality between necessity and perpetuity: necessity is more specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish necessity from perpetuity and explain the absolute generality relationship using their contradictories.

Core Idea
Necessity is narrower than perpetuity: every necessary is perpetual, but not every perpetual is necessary.

In Islamic logic, modal qualifiers like necessity (ḍarūra) and perpetuity (dawām) are crucial for evaluating propositions in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh. This passage from a commentary establishes that necessity is more specific than perpetuity and proves it through the relationship of their contradictories. Understanding this distinction helps prevent errors in modal reasoning about divine attributes and theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Define necessity and perpetuity
Necessity means the predicate is essentially inseparable from the subject (impossible to be false). Perpetuity means the predicate holds at all times, but may not be impossible of failure.
Necessity involves essential impossibility; perpetuity only temporal constancy.
2
Verify necessity implies perpetuity
If a proposition is necessary, it cannot be false at any time, so it must be true at all times. Thus, it is perpetual. Check any counterexample: not possible.
3
Test the converse (perpetuity implies necessity)
Find a perpetual proposition that is not necessary. Example: 'Zayd walks all his life' – always true but could have been false, so not necessary. Therefore, the converse fails.
This shows perpetuity is broader.
4
Analyze the argument from contradictories
The contradictory of necessity (al-mumkinah al-ʿāmmah: possibly false) is more general than the contradictory of perpetuity (al-muṭlaqah al-ʿāmmah: absolute). If necessity and perpetuity were equal, their contradictories would be equal. Since they are not, necessity must be more specific.
This is a proof by contraposition using logical relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصله ان النسبة بينهما هي العموم المطلق فانه كلما صدقت الضرورة صدق الدوام من غير عكس فان الدوام قد يكون غير مستحيل فلا يكون ضرورة البتة فانها لابد وان يكون مستحلية فان المراد من الاستحالة الاستحالة الناشئة ذات الموضوع لا المطلقة الناشئة منها او من غيرها والالكانت الضرورية والدائمة متساويتين وهو باطل قطعاً فان نقيض الضرورية و هو الممكنة العامة اعم من نقيض الدائمة و هو المطلقة العامة كما صرح به غير واحد ولو كانتا متساويتين تلزم ان يكون نقيضا الاعم والاخص مطلقا متساويين وقد سبق ان بين نقيضيها ايضاً عموماً مطلقا لكن بعكس العينين. وبعبارة اخرى أوضح من ذلك لو كانتا متساويتين للزم ان يكون نقيضا هما ايضاً متساويين على ما سبق تحقيقه والحال أن بينهما عموماً مطلقا كما ذكر. نعم قد يطلق الضرورة على استحالة انفكاك شيء عن شيء مطلقا فتسمى الضرورة بالمعنى الاعم كالاول بالمعنى الاخص لكن ليس ذلك بمرادهم فى مبحث القضاياء بدليل ذكر هم الدوام في مقابله و الى ما ذكرنا اشار المحشى حيث قال: «و ان لم يكن مستحيلاً». (میرزا محمد علی)
The gist is that the relationship between them is absolute generality, for whenever necessity is true, perpetuity is true, but not conversely. For perpetuity may be non-impossible and thus not necessity at all, because necessity must involve impossibility. For what is meant by impossibility is the impossibility arising from the essence of the subject, not the absolute impossibility arising from it or from something else. Otherwise, the necessary and the perpetual would be equal, which is definitely false. For the contradictory of necessity, which is the general possible, is more general than the contradictory of perpetuity, which is the general absolute, as more than one has stated. If they were equal, then their contradictories would also be equal, but it has preceded that between their contradictories there is also absolute generality, but reversed. And in another clearer expression: if they were equal, their contradictories would also be equal according to what was previously investigated, while the situation is that there is absolute generality between them as mentioned. Yes, necessity may be applied to the impossibility of separation of a thing from a thing absolutely, and then it is called necessity in the broader sense, like the first in the narrower sense, but that is not what is intended by them in the discussion of propositions, as evidenced by their mentioning perpetuity in contrast to it. And the commentator alluded to what we mentioned when he said: 'even if it is not impossible.' (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage establishes the logical relationship between necessity (ḍarūra) and perpetuity (dawām). The author argues that necessity implies perpetuity (every necessary proposition is perpetual), but not vice versa (some perpetual propositions are contingent, not necessary). The reason is that necessity involves impossibility of the opposite (i.e., the predicate cannot fail to hold for the subject), while perpetuity only means the predicate always holds, but not necessarily (it could have failed in some possible world). The author uses the contradictories: the contradictory of necessity is 'possible general' (mumkinah ʿāmmah), which is wider than the contradictory of perpetuity (mutlaqah ʿāmmah). If necessity and perpetuity were equal, their contradictories would be equal, but they are not. This argument is a form of immediate inference using contraposition and the principle that if two terms are coextensive, their negations are also coextensive. The author also notes that sometimes 'necessity' is used in a broader sense to include any impossibility of separation, but in the context of propositions, they distinguish it from perpetuity.

Key Terms
الضرورة
الدوام
العموم المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are equal.
B) The contradictory of necessity is more general than that of perpetuity.
C) The contradictory of perpetuity is more general than that of necessity.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Necessity implies perpetuity, but not conversely; this relationship is proven using the broader scope of the contradictory of necessity.

Understanding 'As Long as the Description' in Conditioned Propositions
Author explains the meaning of 'madaam al-wasf' in general conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the meaning of 'madaam al-wasf' in general conditioned propositions used in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
'As long as the description' means the predicate holds throughout the description's duration, regardless of its causal role.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of 'madaam al-wasf' (as long as the description) within general conditioned propositions. The author clarifies that this expression does not introduce a separate condition but rather specifies the temporal scope of the predicate's permanence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting conditioned propositions in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the term
Identify 'madaam al-wasf' in the passage and note its relation to permanence (dawām).
The passage emphasizes that they did not consider two meanings here.
2
Distinguish the two possible roles of the description
Is the description essential for the predicate (like 'writer' for 'moving fingers') or incidental (like 'writer' for 'animal')? The passage says the truth condition remains the same.
3
Understand the logical implication
The predicate holds for the subject at all times the description exists, regardless of whether the description is the cause.
The author says: 'if the predicate is perpetual for the combination of essence and description, then it is perpetual for the essence in all times of the description.'
4
Apply to examples
Test with your own Islamic example to ensure the condition is correctly applied.
Use examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : مادام الوصف العنواني ثابتاً لتلك الذات»: لا يذهب عليك انهم لم يعتبروا هنا معنيين كما اعتبروا في المشروطة العامة بان يعتبروا الدوام بشرط الوصف و مادام الوصف و ذلك لان الدوام كما ذكر عدم انفكاك الشيء عن الشيء فحينئذ لا يتفاوت الحال بين ان يكون للوصف مدخل في دوام المحمول للموضوع كما في قولنا: (بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً أو لم يكن كما في قولنا : «بالدوام كل كاتب حيوان مادام كاتباً فان المحمول اذا كان دائماً لمجموع الذات والوصف كان دائما للذات في جميع اوقات الوصف فتأمل. (میراز محمد علی)
His saying: 'as long as the titular description is established for that essence': let it not escape you that they did not consider here two meanings as they considered in the general conditioned, that they consider permanence with the condition of the description and as long as the description. This is because permanence, as mentioned, is the non-separation of a thing from a thing. So then the situation does not differ between whether the description has an input in the permanence of the predicate for the subject, as in our saying: 'perpetually, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer', or not, as in our saying: 'perpetually, every writer is an animal as long as he is a writer'. For if the predicate is perpetual for the combination of essence and description, then it is perpetual for the essence in all times of the description. So ponder.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'as long as the description' (madaam al-wasf) in general conditioned propositions (al-mashrūṭah al-ʿāmmah). The author clarifies that they do not treat two separate conditions here (unlike elsewhere). The term 'permanence' (dawām) simply means non-separation of the predicate from the subject. Whether the description contributes to the permanence (e.g., 'writer' is essential for 'moving fingers') or not (e.g., 'writer' is incidental to 'animal'), the predicate still holds for the subject as long as the description holds. The example given: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' – here the predicate 'moving fingers' is dependent on the description 'writer'. In contrast, 'Every writer is an animal as long as he is a writer' – here 'animal' is independent of writing. In both cases, the predicate is true for the subject during all times the description applies, but the reason for permanence differs.

Key Terms
مادام الوصف
الدوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That the predicate holds only when the description is essential.
B) That the predicate holds for the subject at all times the description exists.
C) That the description must be the cause of the predicate.
D) That the predicate holds after the description ends.

Key Takeaway
In general conditioned propositions, 'as long as the description' simply means the predicate is true for the subject throughout the duration of the description, without requiring the description to be the cause.

Refuting the Special Treatment of Negative 'Madaam' Propositions
Refutation of a prior restrictive interpretation of 'madaam'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that only negative 'madaam' propositions imply descriptive permanence, using the principle that common usage does not distinguish between positive and negative.

Core Idea
Common usage understands 'madaam' in positive and negative propositions identically—no special treatment needed.

Some earlier scholars argued that only negative propositions prefixed with 'madaam' (as long as) carry the sense of descriptive permanence (al-dawām al-waṣfī), while positive propositions only imply it if the predicate is modified (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl). The author of this commentary refutes that view, appealing to common linguistic usage (al-ʿurf) and the established rule of negation and affirmation from Sheikh Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani. In this lesson, you will learn to dissect this critique and apply the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opposing View
Find the claim that some scholars made: that common usage (al-ʿurf) understands descriptive permanence only from negative propositions (al-sāliba), or from positive propositions only when the predicate is modified (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl). This is the target of the critique.
The passage mentions some investigators (baʿḍ al-muḥaqqiqīn) who held this view.
2
Locate the Author's Refutation
The author states that this claim 'is not good' (laysa bi-jayyid). He argues that common usage understands this meaning from the negative just as it understands it from the positive, so there is no need to restrict it to negatives.
Key phrase: 'فان العرف كما يفهم هذا المعنى من السالبة فكذا يفهم من الموجبة أيضاً'.
3
Examine the Counterexamples
The author gives examples: 'every sleeper is not awake' (positive, predicate modified) and 'nothing of the sleeper is awake' (negative). He argues that common usage understands descriptive permanence in both, even without modified predicates.
Note that the positive example 'كل نائم غير مستيقظ' has a modified predicate, but the author insists even unmodified positives work.
4
Understand the Underlying Rule
The author cites the rule from Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani: when negation enters a statement with a restriction, it addresses that restriction, and similarly for affirmation. This rule applies equally to both positive and negative propositions.
This rule from 'Dala'il al-I'jaz' is the linguistic basis for the parity.
5
Apply the Rule to Both Cases
Test the rule: for 'nothing of the sleeper is awake', the negation negates 'awake' from the sleeper during sleep. For 'every sleeper is not awake', the affirmation affirms the negation during sleep. Both yield descriptive permanence.
The author concludes that there is no distinction between positive and negative in the understanding of 'madaam'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «بل من الموجبة ايضاً »: اشارة الى ان ما يستفاد من بعض المحققين في شرح الرسالة وغيره من تخصيص فهم العرف هذا المعنى بالسالبة، ليس بجيد فان العرف كما يفهم هذا المعنى من السالبة فكذا يفهم من الموجبة أيضاً فلا حاجة لتخصيصه بالسالبة. و في اطلاق الموجبة و عدم تقييدها بمعدولة المحمول و كذا فى اطلاق السائبة و عدم تقييدها بكونها غير معدولة المحمول اشارة الى ان ماربما يتوهم هنا كما اتفق لبعض المحققين من المحشين في شرح الكلام من ان فهم العرف هذا المعنى و ان كان غير مختص بالسالبة بل كما يفهم منها، يفهم من الموجبة أيضاً لكن لا مطلقا بل اذا كانت فى معنى السالبة كما اذا كانت معدولة المحمول كقولنا : «كل نائم غير مستيقظ» و كذا ليس في مطلق السالبة، بل اذا لم تكن في معنى الموجبة والا كما اذا كانت معدولة المحمول مثل قولنا : «لاشيء من الكاتب بلا حيوان فلا يفهم هذا المعنى ، ليس كما ينبغى بل العرف لا يفرق بين الموجبة و السالبة المعدولة المحمول و بين غير هما فى فهم هذا المعنى ويفهمه في الموجبة و ان لم تكن معدولة المحمول وفى السالبة و ان كانت معدولة كما هو ظاهر، هذا. واظن ان الذي أوقعهم في ذلك ظهور فهم هذا المعنى في السائبة بناء على القاعدة المقررة عند ارباب المعانى المذكورة آنفاً فاته اذا قلنا: لاشيء من النائم بمستيقظ فالنفى يفيد سلب المستيقظ من النائم وثبوته للشخص وذلك معنى الدوام الوصفى وخفائه فى الموجبة بناء على توهم عدم ثبوت تلك القاعدة فيها وانت خبير بان هذه القاعدة و ان كانت مشهورة فى النقي الا ان الاثبات ايضاً كذلك. قال الشيخ عبد القاهر البيانى فى دلائل الاعجاز ان النفى اذادخل على كلام فيه تقييد بوجه ما يتوجه الى ذلك القيد وكذلك الاثبات وجملة الامر انه ما من كلام فيه امر زايد على مجرد اثبات الشيء
His saying: 'but also from the positive': an indication that what is understood from some investigators in the commentary on the Risala and others, that the common understanding (al-ʿurf) of this meaning is specific to the negative, is not good. For the common understanding understands this meaning from the negative just as it understands it from the positive, so there is no need to specify it for the negative. And in the absolute use of the positive without restriction to the modified predicate, and similarly in the absolute use of the negative without restriction to its being unmodified predicate, there is an indication that what might be imagined here, as happened to some investigators among the commentators in the commentary on the speech, that the common understanding of this meaning, although not specific to the negative, but is understood from the positive as well, but not absolutely, rather when it is in the meaning of the negative, as when the predicate is modified (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl) like our saying: 'every sleeper is not awake', and similarly not in the absolute negative, rather when it is not in the meaning of the positive, otherwise as when the predicate is modified like our saying: 'nothing of the writer is without animal', then this meaning is not understood. This is not as it should be; rather the common understanding does not distinguish between the positive and the negative with modified predicate and between others in understanding this meaning, and understands it in the positive even if the predicate is not modified, and in the negative even if it is modified, as is apparent. This. And I think that what caused them to fall into this is the apparent understanding of this meaning in the negative based on the established rule among the masters of meanings mentioned earlier. For when we say: 'nothing of the sleeper is awake', the negation indicates the negation of 'awake' from the sleeper and its affirmation for the person? That is the meaning of descriptive permanence. And its hiddenness in the positive is based on the illusion that that rule is not established there. And you are aware that this rule, although famous in negation, is also the same in affirmation. Sheikh Abd al-Qahir al-Bayyani said in Dala'il al-I'jaz: 'When negation enters a speech that contains a restriction in some way, it addresses that restriction, and similarly affirmation.' In short, there is no speech that contains an additional element beyond mere affirmation of the thing.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous scholar's view on how common usage (al-ʿurf) understands 'descriptive permanence' (al-dawām al-waṣfī) in propositions using 'mā dāma' (as long as). The author (Mirza Muhammad Ali) argues that some investigators claimed that only negative propositions (e.g., 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake as long as it sleeps') have the understood meaning of permanence during the description, while positive propositions (e.g., 'Every sleeper is not awake as long as it sleeps') only imply that if the predicate is modified (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl). The author refutes this, stating that the common understanding does not distinguish between positive and negative; both are understood in the same way. He provides examples: 'nothing of the sleeper is awake' implies that 'awake' is negated from the sleeper during sleep, and similarly 'every sleeper is not awake' affirms the negation during sleep. The rule from Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani is that negation (and similarly affirmation) addresses any restriction in the statement. Therefore, the 'urf understands this meaning in both positive and negative propositions without restriction to modified predicates.

Key Terms
العرف
الدوام الوصفي
معدولة المحمول

C) Only if the proposition is negative.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic common sense treats positive and negative 'madaam' propositions as equally conveying descriptive permanence, so no special condition like a modified predicate is needed for positives.
